This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission that visited
the Central African Republic in March/April 1987. The mission members were
Christopher Shaw (mission leader, educator); Bernard Abeille (architect);
FranSoise Delannoy (project officer); and Fran'ois Orivel (economist,
consultant). This report was drafted by the mission members and completed
by 1Hr. Shaw. Mmes. Ann Joseph and Micheline Thibaud assisted in processing
the report.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
CREDIT AND PROJECT SUNMARY
Borrower:      Central African Republic
Beneficiarv:   Ministry of National Education;
Ministry of Higher Education and Scientific Research; and
National Scholarship Service
IDA Credit
Amount:        SDR 14.1 million (US$18.0 million equivalent)
Terms:         Standard with 40 years maturity
Pro ect
Description:   The project would support the Government's education strategy
within its structural adjustment framework, by reviewing
annually an action plan containing sector budgetary targets,
the scholarship program and budget, and staffing and
pedagogical targets. Specifically, the project aims to:
I.   Rehabilitate the Education System for Improved Quality
and Efficiency in:
1.   Primary schooling by:
(a) raising teacher quality nationwide through
workshops and in-service training for 120
trainers, pedagogical advisors and inspectors;
and about 3,500 teachers and school directors;
(b) improving learning conditions through: (i) a
national pedagogical *aaterials program to
supply about 430,000 books and teachers'
guides; and (ii) a regionally phased school
rehabilitation and maintenance program for
about 960 classrooms, both while encouraging
community participation; and
(c) strengthening the pedagogical support/super-
visory functions nationwide through the
reinforcement of 3 regional academic
inspectorates and the provision of basic
transportation and related operating costs for
primary school inspectorates.



- vi -
2. Higher education at the University of Bangui by:
(a) improving study facilities through a university
library extension and the provision of 7,500
additional books, and the rehabilitation of the
science faculty building and provision of
essential laboratory equipment and supplies.
II. Imgrove Sector Management for a More Effective Use of
Resources in:
1.  the Ministry of National Education (MEN) by:
(a) strengthening its capacity to plan, allocate
and control: (i) personnel, and (ii) financial
and physical resources through office
improvements, data processing, training, and
specialist services for MEN's key Directorates;
and
(b) reinforcing its implementation capacity through
the creation of a project coordination unit.
2.   Higher education by:
(a) promoting rationalization and reduction of
expenditures on scholarships through the devel-
opment of the National Scholarship Service; and
(b) reinforcing the planning capacity at the
University of Bangui.

Project Benefits
and Riskst        The project will promote improved quality and relevance of
inputs and better resource management, resulting in more
efficient operation, reduced costs per graduate, and
increased pupil achievement. At the primary level, for an
increase in costs per student of 15% (2,250 CFAF), it is
expected that these improvements would translate into a
reduction of the dropout and repeater rates by as much as
30%, and a 40Z decline in the costs per graduate. The
project will ensure a closer qualitative and quantitative
match between the output of the education system and the
needs of the economy especially at the post-secondary
level. A more efficient allocation of resources, combined
with better management, will support, at the sector level,
the Government's structural adjustment program. The
project has two main risks. First, the CAR has no
experience of complex sectoral projects in the field of
education. To ensure that the sector budgetary targets set
out in the ActB n Plan are acted upon, the Ministry of
Economy, Finance and Planning, would participate in the
annual review process. In addition, implementation
problems arising from the complexity of the project are
addressed by staggering the actions envisaged for both
institutional development and the physical rehabilitation
of schools. Second, at present the CAR education sector
suffers from weak financial management, planning and
budgeting, and ongoing projects encounter delays in the
provision of counterpart funds. The project has been
designed to alleviate this situation with a carefully
planned approach of Government to sector rehabilitation; by
the increased participation of local communities with labor
and local inputs; by short-term consultancy services in
financial management; and by the training program for the
national team in project management.



CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.1       The Government of the Central Africa Republic (CAR) has requested
IDA assistance in financing a project to improve the quality of the education
sector, together with its managerial capacity and financing structure. Total
project costs are estimated at US$20.7 million equivalent (net of taxes and
duties), with a foreign exchange component of US$11.9 million. The Govern-
ment would contribute US$1.3 million, parental associations would contribute
in kind (labor or materials) or through cash the equivalent of US$1.2
million, other donors (UNDP) would cofinance with US$0.1 million; and the
remaining U£$18.0 million would be financed by IDA.
1.2       The project Is consistent with the CAR's structural adjustment
program (financed by Cr. 1732-CA and Cr. A23-CA) and provides support by
introducing expenditure control on scholarships, improving resource
management, and extending the public administration reform to the education
sector. The policy measures proposed in education were developed through a
sector policy dialogue conducted with the CaR authorities during the past
eighteen months. Annex 1 provides the Basic Data.
II. THE EDUCATION SECTOR
A.   Overview
2.1       The education system consists of: (a) a six-year primary cycle;
(b) four years of lower, and three years of upper, secondary schooling; and
(c) higher education at the University of Bangui or aDroad. Teachers are
trained at the post-secondary level. The country also has a network of ten
vocational training centers. Since 1986, sectoral responsibility has been
split between: the Ministry of National Education (MEN) for formal education
(general and technical) through the secondary level; the Ministry of Higher
Education and Scientific Research (iESRES) for the ,ertiary level; the
Ministry of Civil Service and Labor for vocational training; and the techni-
cal ministries for their own training programs. Administrative authority for
primary and secondary education is decentralized to Academic Inspectorates
(IAs) located in the regions. Annex 2-1 shows the structure of the education
system, and Annex 2-2, the organization charts of MEN and MESRES.
B. Main Issues in Educational Development ^
2.2       The CAR's education system has achieved wide coverage at moderate
costs. The adult literacy rate in 1985 was estimated at 40X, while primary
enrollments (of which 36% are girls) grew annually by 5.82 in 1980-85,
bringing the gross enrollment ratio to 78%. At 15,000 CFAF, or 18% of GDP
p.c., unit costs per pupil/year are below the average for francophone Africa



- 2 -
(23%). Private schooling at the primary level is virtually negAigible and
accounts for only 8% )f enrollments at the secondary level.  However, these
features are largely offset by three critical issues: (a) poor quality and
low internal efficiency; (b) weak management and planning capacity; and
(c) poor resource allocation exacerbated by severe financial constraints.
Annex 2-3 shows the statistical profile of the sector, and Annex 2-4 gives
educative comparative data.
1.   Poor Quality and Low Internal Efficiency
2.3       The most critical issue Zacing education is its poor quality and
low internal efficiency, especially at the primary level. The underlying
factors are: (a) the inadequacy of teachers' basic and professional skills
(despite the recent revision of the teachers' profile), aggravated by the
absence of in-service training; (b) the lack of experience of school direc-
tors in school and teacher managenent; (c) the dearth of pedagogic materials,
textbooks are scarce, expensive and their production specifications are high;
(d) inefficient or high-cost building techrniques combined with an absence of
repairs or preventive maintenance which have led to a deteriorating school
environment where 60% of primary classrocmis (permanent structures) require
major repairs while the remaining 40% are temporary structures needing
replacement; (e) the inability of inspectorate staff to provide adequate
control and pedagogic support, owing to lack of operating means and up-to-
date knowledge of modern teaching methods; and (f) large average class-sizes
due to inefficient school management and timetables. Understandably, little
student achievement takes place: with average repeater and dropout rates of
about 33% and 12%, only about 21% of an initial cohort eventually graduates
from primary school. Thus, with poor learning conditions and limited selec-
tivity, it takes 24 years of instruction to produce a primary education
graduate (vs. a theoretical six years), plaring the CAR education system
among the least efficient in the world. Qualitative problems continue as
students progress up the educational ladder, producing unit costs per
graduate equivalent to 4.4, 18, 48 and 120 times GNP p.c. at the primary,
secondary general, secondary technical and university levels. At the secon-
dary level, while the number of examination candidates at the junior and
senior secondary examinations have increased by 53% and 85% respectively over
the last six years, selectivity has been strengthened and the success rates
have declined by over half so that the numbers of successful candidates are
either stable or slightly less than six years ago. Over 70% of secondary
school leavers have their diplomas in humanities-based abJects. At the
university level, student achievement is hampered by: (i) poor study faci-
lities and limited library space and books for all subject areas; and
(ii) limited facilities for experimental work in science based subjects. The
library - with one place for every 25 students - is well organized and
managed, but overcrowded and has an aged and limited collection of works.
The stock of science equipment has not been renewed since its original
acquisition in 1970. Annex 2-5 provides details on Internal Efficiency,
Quality and Student Achievement, and Annex 2-6 on Primary School Investment
Costs.



2.   Weak Sector Management and Planning Capacity
2.4       The administrative structure of the formal education system with
two separate ministries -- MEN and MESRES -- is a common feature of the West
African francophor.e countries, and allows simplification of the management of
the system, without the creation of a single, unwieldy department. Giver. the
current levels of management expertise, this arrangement has clear advantages
and is well suited to the CAR, although MEN employs over one-third of the
civil service and manages about one-fourth of the national budget, it is not
equipped to use these resources effectively. This is due to several factors.
First, while the revised structure and decentralization of MEN in January
1986 together with the staffing plan, completed in April 1987, provided a
sound basis for correcting weaknesses and identified redundant posts in MEN,
personnel management has been poor, resulting in an excessive proportion of
non-teachers in the sector, especially in secondary education. Second, this
has been compounded by the lack of coherent, economically sound, and
systematically applied financial procedures, combined with poor investment
planning and programming. Third, MEN's weak capacity in physical planning
and resource management, as well as the sparse, fragmented and unreliable
data on the sector, has carried a high cost in terms of remedial maintenance,
and suboptimal use and expansion of facilities. Fourth, the poor data base,
especially with regard to labor statistics, is compounded by limited capa-
bilities for the collection and analysis of educational statistics. This
ooscures the linkage of the sector with the labor market, and weakens MEN's
ability to ensure a qualitative and quantitative match between demand and
supply at the least cost. Finally, project implementation has been defi-
cient, suffering from staff turnover and the scarcity of experienced managers
and accountants.
3.   Financial Constraints and Poor Resource Allocation
2.5       Budget Constraints.  The Government's commitment to education is
reflected in the share of total public recurrent spending allocated to the
sector -- 24.7% in 1986 -- which, despite a recsnt decline, is higher than
the median for francophone Africa (21.7%). These resources are complemented
by: (a) various private expenditures, equivalent to 7.7% of public
educational expenditures, including a modest 0.5% in contributions made by
parents' associations (APEs); and (b) external support to the recurrent
budget (from the French), used for expatriate teachers' salaries and
materials for secondary schools. With school-age population projected to
grow at 3% p.a. and public recurrent expenditure to fall by 9% in real terms
over the next six years, additional public resources will not be available to
maintain the existing primary enrollment ratio while raising educational
quality. Therefore, financing the necessary pedagogic inputs (in-service
teacher training, textbooks, and increased supervision) will require a
stabilization of the share of the education budget, a widening of the
sector's resource base and more rational and systematic budgetary procedures.
2.6       Skewed expenditure pattern.  Not only are resources tightly con-
strained, but their distribution by level of education and by types of expen-
diture is inadequate, resulting in inequity and inefficiency. For lack of
well-defined allocating criteria and mechanisms, reflecting a long-term



- 4 -
global strategy and clear priorities, the recurrent budget structure by level
has been distorted in favor of higher education. The share of higher educa-
tion, accounting for less than 1% of the students, has risen from 22% in 1982
to 24% in 1986 of total education budgetary resources; that for primary
education, with 83% of the students, fell from 63% in 1982 to 49% in 1986.
This bias is inefficient, both because social rates of return, in CAR as
elsewhere, are much greater in primary '23Z-50% range) than in higher
education (17.5%) and because it has enaourage' students to pursue their
studies beyond economically justified levzla, affecting the quality of
instruction and resulting in graduate unemployment or employment in an
already saturated civil service. Over the same period, secondary education
expenditures have doubled from 8% to 17%, while central administration and
other expenditures have remained close to 10% of the total expenditures.
Annex 2-7 presents an analysis of returns to education in CAR.
2.7       In addition, the spending pattern by nature is distorted in favor
of non-pedagogically efficient inputs. The education sector investment
budget has focused on expansion of the school network (externally financed)
while neglecting regular renewal (Government financed). Its recurrent budget
has also been unbalanced. First, the share of salary expenditures has been
dominant (98% for primary in 1985).  This is not due to high salaries (they
have been frozen since 1982, resulting in serious teacher demotivation and
lack of discipline), but to staff numbers in non-teaching positions (11% of
total staff or 7% at the primary level) and limited non-salary expenditures.
Second, the share of scholarships amounted to 67% of higher education
expenditures or 16% of the total education sector budget in 1985 (see
detailed discussion in para 2.8). Third, at all levels of education, peda-
gogic materials (text and exercise books) and other operating/maintenance
expenses, which are the lifeblood of the sector, have been treated as a
residual and received only 2% of the sector budget (an overestimate, since it
includes student restaurants which are of little pedagogic value). Although
not uncommon in Africa, this is below the minimum required to ensure effec-
tive use of the teaching staff. As a result, the ratio of books/students has
fallen to 1:20, and schools have been left unsupervised, making the system
inefficient. Moreover, for lack of budgetary controls, implementation of
these uncoordinated decisions has never been monitored, making it impossible
to trace expenditures, measure the efficiency of the system, or make specific
recommendations for improvements. It is therefore clear that the corrective
measures taken in 1987, with the revision of the budgetary nomenclature,
require reinforcement if Government's objectives for the sector are to be
attained. Annex 2-3 provides background information on the evolution of
MEN's budget by level of education and type of expenditure, Annex 2-8 on
teacher salaries, and Annex 2-9 on the Financial Implications of Educational
Policies.
2.8       The scholarship issue is serious enough to warrant a separate
discussion. In CAR as elsewhere in Africa, due to loosely defined and
applied eligibility criteria, scholarships have promoted neither academic
excellence nor the qualitative and quantitative match with the labor market.
Because scholarships are not used as a selection/orientation mechanism,
h'igher education facilities are overcrowded, and the vast majority of Stu-
dents continue to choose liberal arts (82% in 1985) where employment pro-
spects are limited, over scientific and technical fields, for which there is
an unmet demand. A CAR characteristic, however, is that 57% of scholarship



-5-
expenditure goes to students abroad, who only represent 29% of the total
number. This policy has been costly in more ways than ones it has encour-
aged young people to study abroad, sometimes for disciplines taught in
Bangui; given the weak selection criteria and poor management, not surpris-
ingly many fail or never return, contributing to the low social return on
Government investment. This policy has also starved the University of Bangui
of the resources (books for libraries, laboratory equipment, maintenance,
etc.) critically needed to offer quality education to those remaining in the
country. Simulations have shown that uncontrolled growth in scholarship
expenditures would drive total education spending beyond 31% of the Govern-
ment budget, negating every possible attempt to improve quality. Actions to
correct this trend, with a review of scholarship rolls started in January
1987 and the abandonment of a policy of guaranteed government employment, tre
an important aspect of Government's efforte to improve its macro-economic
management as per the SAL.
C. The Government's Sector Strategy
2.9       The Government has a two-pronged education strategy aimed for the
long term att (a) improving quality (starting at the primary level) while
increasing relevance to the needs of CAR's economy and society; and (b)
strengthening the management and administration of the sector through
decentralization while mobilizing the support of all the parties involved.
With respect to quality improvements, the Government requested IDA's
assistance in improving the operation of the system, as a prerequisite to a
reform in educational contents and methods. With respect to the training/
employment linkage, the Government proposes to: (i) develop a global frame-
work covering pre- and post-secondary technical education, and vocational
training; (ii) focus on coordination and planning functions; and
(iii) rehabilitate the existing vocational training network in connection
with the needs of individual regions. This strategy makes sense in that it
addresses the key issues in a rational way, allowing for a gradual lifting of
the present financial, human and physical constraints. It therefore deserves
IDA's full support, and has guided the design of the proposed project
(para 3.1). Annex 2-10 details the Government's Sector Policy.
D.   The Bank Group's Role
2.10      Since 1969, 13 IDA credits, two supplemental credits and two
special African facility credits) totalling just over US$151 millions equiva-
lent have been approved for the CAR. Of these, two credits and one supple-
mental credit were for education. The first education project (Cr. 308-CA
for US$3.9 million, effective in 1973 and completed in 1981 with supplementa-
ry credit Cr. 1073-CA for US$0.9 million) supported secondary education and
aimed at providing trained and qualified manpower for the modern sector.
Implemented under difficult conditions, the scope of the project was reduced,
and while quantitative targets were reached, qualitative improvements were
only partially achieved and policy Issues of resource allocation were not
addressed, The ongoing second project (Cr. 1359-CA for US$11.0 million,
effective in 1983) aims at correcting the neglect suffered by primary edu-
cation during the previous decade and improving administration, planning,
evaluation and research where poor quality had hampered balaaced educational
development, but once again does not address the resotmrce allocation or
efficiency issues. Physical components of primary classroom rehabilitation



and maintenance were limited geographically, have progressed well and their
modification and improvement provides the basis for a similar program in new
zones. However, high staff turnover, and weak consultants have led to
disappointing results in the planning component, while poor project manage-
ment, limited counterpart funds, and inefficient financial control have
slowed down implementation and delayed the evaluation component. These weak-
nesses would be corrected under the proposed third project as part of the
strategy mentioned in para 2.9.
2.11      The thrust of IDA's country strategy is the provision of coherent
and consistent support to maximize the impact of the Government's structural
adjustment program. IDA has been asked to help the Government define a
global and long-term framework for the rehabilitation and development of the
education sector, and to identify the policy reforms necessary for
cost-effective resource allocation and investment in education. In this
project, IDA would play a catalytic role in helping to provide this policy
and i..stitutional framework for cost-effective resource allocation and would
help coordinate other aid efforts, primarily through improvements in sector
resource planning, budgeting and management. So far, IDA's assistance has
been instrumental in providing a framework for an educational rehabilitation
program which has guided the actions of other agencies and is coherent with
the activities of the major bilateral donor; France. Continuation of this
involvement through policy advice to follow-up the June 1987 Round Table
meeting is important for ensuring a balanced development of the sector
coherent with the Government's structural adjustment program. Actions to
improve the selectivity and relevance of secondary education are being
undertaken by the UNDP and the French, while actions to tighten the
education/employment linkage are being undertaken by several other agencies
(ILO in institution-building, EDF in planning, AfDB and OPEC in vocational
and technical facilities rehabilitation). Therefore IDA's direct role in
this area would be limited to the development and monitoring of a long-term
global framework for relating employment and training, and the financing of
specific studies to fill certain gaps.
III. THE PROJECT
A.   Project Objectives and Description
3.1       The project would support the implementation of the Government's
educational strategy (para. 2.9) in the context of its structural adjustment
program. To ensure that sufficient resources are available for the
qualitative improvements and increased managerial capacity which are the
project's key objectives, and these resources are optimally allocated; during
negotiations, IDA and the Government discussed and agreed upon a three-year
rolling Action Plan, to be monitored each year during September at a ioint
Government/IDA review. The joint Government/IDA review would examine the
action plan, its interim annual targets, the education sector recurrent
budget, the public investment program for education and progress on project
implementation. It would be related to the review of the public investment
program and public expenditure review undertaken in the framework of the SAL.



- 7 -
ACTION PLAN
The sector budgetary targets (which would have annual intorim values fixod during the
joint review) would aim at: (a) monitoring annually tho proportion of the Government
recurrent budget allocated to the education sector; (b) raising expenditures on
prlmary education froe 62% to at loset 86%, and on materials froe 2# to at least 8% of
the oducation recurrent budgot by 1994; and (c) agreeing with IDA annually on the
composition of tho education investment budget for the coming three years.
The Scholarship program and budget would aim at: (d) reducing Covernment expenditure
on scholarships from 16% to 8% of the education recurrent budg'- during the project
life; and (e) focusing Government funded overseas scholarships on key scientific,
technological and managerial fields not available at the University of Bangui.
Staffing targets would aim at: (f) reducing the proportion of non-teachers in the
total oducation staff from 11X to 7% by 1993 through staff redeployment. In addition,
the joint Covernment/IDA annual review would examine project Implementation
(specifically: the In-service training plan; the personnel plan and the annual
claseroom rehabilitation and maintenance program).
Continued disbursement under the credit would be conditional upon agreement
each year between the Government and IDA on the contents of the Action Plan
and the satisfactory joint annual review of progress made in implementing
this action plan. Annex 3-1 provides details of the Action Plan, and
Annex 2-9 simulates the impact of the above financial policies on the primary
enrollment level.
3.2        The project is specifically designed to help:  (a) rehabilitate the
education system for improved quality and efficiency of primary and higher
education; and (b) improve sector management for a more effective use of
resources in the Ministry of National Education and higher education. These
major project components are discussed as follows.
1.   Rehabilitation of the System for Improved Quality and Efficiency of
Primary and Higher Education
3.3        To rehabilitate the education system for improved quality and
efficiency of both primary and higher education, the project woulds
(a) raise teacher quality nationwide at the primary level through an
in-service training program; (b) improve learning conditions through an
increase in the supply of pedagogical materials in all primary schools and
the rehabilitation of the school environment; (c) support and develop academ-
ic and primary inspectorates; and (d) improve university study facilities
through an extension of the library and renovation of the science faculty.
3.4        Primary Teacher Quality.  In order to restore the delivery of edu-
cation to acceptable cost-effective standards, the project would improve
basic and professional teaching skills of the whole teaching corps.  F;rst,
in PYI a program of workshops, seminars, and task forces would be organized
at the central level for: (a) teacher trainers, to adapt pre-service train-
ing curricula to the new definition of the teachers' profile and role
(para 2.3); (b) inspectors and pedagogical advisors, to improve teacher
supervision and management, and prepare them to conduct the in-service



training program per se; and (c) MEN's key pedagogic and personnel staff, to
formulate detailed proposals to Fmprove teachers' career stream and the use
of human and physical resources. Second, as of PY2, the in-service program
itself would start in the three project regions and extend to two more
regions respectively in PY3 and PY4. The program would be conducted by the
inspectors and pedagogic advisors in the seven regional pedagogy centers
(CPRs), plus the National In-service Training Center (CNFC). It would
consist annually, of one three-week session for school directors and three
modular sessions for the teachers. The program would reach all inspec-
tors/pedagogic advisors, about 84% of school directors, and 58% of primary
teachers. The subjects covered would echo key themes of the project
(including health education), would be related to rural skills and the CAR's
economy and society, and would be reinforced by biweekly radio broadcasts, an
experimental correspondence in-service training course and a pedagogic
liaison bulletin.
3.5       The credit would finance:  (a) furniture, vehicles and equipment
for the existing CPRs and pedagogical supplies for the trainees; (b) about 36
manmonths of local and international specialist services for developing and
delivering the above programs; (c) a total of 24 manmonths of scholarships
abroad and a study trip abroad for the trainers; (d) per diem and travel for
all participants; and (e) selected operating costs on a declining basis.
Annual training plans and budgets will be agreed upon at the joint yearly
review. Details are given in Annex 3-2.
3.6       Pedagogical Materials.  In order to improve learning conditions the
project would: (a) ensure an adequate supply of pedagogically relevant
textbooks, at the lowest possible cost, with an immediate initial supply of
textbooks; and (b) develop MEN's publishing capacity to prepare for subse-
quent provision of low-cost, pedagogical materials and supplies. A core
functional package of about 430,000 books and teachers' guides would be
purchased, using competitive bidding and simplified production specifications
to reduce unit costs, thereby achieving an average ratio of one took per two
pupils in reading, mathematics and basic science. The books would be bought
and distributed by MEN to primary schools for loan to students. The local
parents' associations (APEs) would be encouraged to purchase supplements of
materials and books above and beyond the quantities and subject areas
provided in the core package. Storage facilities at the central, inspec-
torate and school levels would be improved and regulations instituted. In
addition, the project would reinforce MEN's capacity to develop -- with
writing, editing, and publishing skills -- evaluate, purchase and distribute
low-cost educational books by strengthening the textbook section at the
National Pedagogical Institute (INRAP).
3.7       The credit would finance:  (a) the renovation of central and
regional storage facilities; (b) furniture and equipment for INRAP; (c) 27
manmonths of specialist services and 50 manmonths of training abroad in book
development, adaptation, publishing, purchase and evaluation techniques;
(d) pedagogical materials, and teachers' guides for all of the tountry's
primary schools; and (e) selected incremental operating costs. Annex 3-3
provides details.
3.8       Rehabilitation of the primary school environment.  To stop the
deterioration of the school environment, maintain the value of existing



- 9 -
investments, and alleviate the strain on the Government budget, the project
would use low-cost designs and construction techniques relying largely on
local materials and labor to introduces (a) a phased reRional progreA of
classroom renovation and replacement; and (b) a primary school preventive
maintenance system. The program for about 880 classrooms would be limited to
three zones (Ouaka, Bangui and Mbomou) identified by Government, through
school mapping (see map) and would renovate about 520 classrooms, replace
about 240 temporary classrooms and construct about 120 classrooms. For new
classrooms, the construction techniques and use of local materials and labor
would reduce the average annual amortized cost/pupil place from US$25 to
US$10 (1987 prices), and the investment cost/pupil place from US$412 to
US$152 (1987 prices); 30Z of this cost would be transferred to local
communities.
3.9       The program would comprise as a first phase, a core package of
about 300 (Ouaka) and 150 (Bangui) classrooms; and as a second phase --
depending on the estimated community contributive capacity, the potential for
cost reduction and the replicability of the first phase -- the rehabilitation
of 160 (Bangui) and 270 (Mbomou) classrooms. Rehabilitation would be
organized by the regional school construction and equipment unit (URCESs
consisting of one international volunteer and one national expert) using
local communities and students' parent associations (APEs), under the
administrative supervision of the academic inspectorates and the technical
supervision of MEN's Directorate of School Construction and Equipment (DCES).
This directorate will be reorganized to include a facility planning unit
responsible for maintaining and managing existing infrastructure and planning
and promoting physical expansion through increased local government and
community participation. The APEs and/or communities would provide local
inputs (artisanal bricks or blocks of stabilized earth, unskilled labor,
sanitary facilities and fencing), the project would contract for the
complementary building elements, locally manufactured furniture, and
classroom supplies. The Government would finance housing for the school
director at the project schools.    Details are provided in Annex 3-4.
3.10      The credit would finance:  (a) civil works contracts for class-
rooms; (b) furniture and pedagogical materials for the same classrooms;
(c) 10 manmonths of fellowships for DCES personnel; (d) 20 days of observa-
tion visits; (e) specialist services (2 manmonths) to establish appropriate
standards for school construction and specialist services (3 manmonths) to
assist the DCES in monitoring program achievements; 72 manmonths of volun-
teers and 468 manmonths of national experts for the DCES and URCES; and
2 workshops for start-up activities and village sensibilization; (f) equip-
ment and vehicles; (g) volunteers and national experts housing; and (h) a
proportion of the incremental operating costs on a declining basis (excluding
teachers' salaries). At negotiations, the Government agreed that: (i) the
project classrooms would be located at existing schools, agreed with IDA, in
Ouaka, Bangui and Mbomou; and (ii) the content of the annual rehabilitation
program would be agreed upon at the joint annual review.
3.11      To correct the lack of maintenance, and the absence of user or
community participation in school upkeep, the project would test, within the
three project zones, a community based primary school preventive maintenance
system, to transfer the responsibility for regular maintenance and minor
repairs to local communities. Appropriate principles and operating



- 10 -
procedures would be developed, involving school-based communities, to be
financed and operated jointly by parents' associations (APEs), school and
local authorities. Preventive maintenance would be organized by a school
committee and URCES, while repairs of accidental damage would be discussed
with the URCES and regional authorities to plan possible government
contribution. Details are provided in Annex 3-4.
3.12      The credit would finance:  (a) two manmonths of specialist services
for training sessions; (b) study visits and ten manmonths of training for the
national experts; regional seminars for school directors, regional authori-
ties and school committees; and workshops for trainers; (c) the development
and printing of 3,000 maintenance guides; (d) the provision of 214 tool kits
for schools; and (e) building materials and craftsman services limited to
repairs of accidental damage for 80 classrooms over a four-year period.
During negotiations, the Govericment agreed that the school maintenance system
and guides will be reviewed with IDA and introduced not later than Septem-
ber 30, 1989, and that maintenance will be adequately supervised by the
academic inspectorates of Banguit Bambari and Bangassou.
3.13      Inspectorates.  In order to develop and strengthen the pedagogical
support, control and advisory functions nationwide, the project would
strengthen the inspectorates in three ways. First, in the project zones, it
would reinforce the decentralizatiou of MEN to its regional offices with
minor civil works, equipment and incremental operating expenses for the three
academic inspectorates at Bambari, Bangui Ville and Bangassou. Second, it
would provide in-service training for primary inspectors, to prepare them to
play their role under a more decentralized system and to upgrade their skills
ins (a) personnel, financial, and school management; (b) educational
administration and planning; (c) pedagogical support to teachers; and
(d) academic supervision. Third, the project would provide transportation
for the primary inspectorates to allow regular school inspection, including
monitoring of students repetition. Motorcycles and mobylettes would be
provided to each primary inspectorate, while small 4x4 vehicles would be
available for primary inspectors on demand from the academic inspectorate.
These improvements in school supervision, together with in-service training
of school directors (pars. 3.4) will promote a reorganization of the school
timetable, double-shift schooling, and better utilization of teachers, with a
view to reducing average class-size from 62 to 55 by 1993/94.
3.14      The credit would finance:  (a) minor civil works and related
furniture and equipment for three academic inspectorates; (b) 18 vehicles and
38 light motocycles; (c) six manmonths of local specialist services to
develop and deliver the training program; (d) local training; and
(e) selected related incremental operating costs on a declining basis.
Details are given in Annex 3-5.
3.15      University Library and Science Faculty.  To address the over-
crowding and scarcity of reading materials at the university library and to
improve the conditions for experimental work in scientific disciplines, the
project would support the physical extension of the library building for both
storage and reading and would repair and re-equip laboratories. An immediate
supply of 2,500 textbooks selected from common texts used at the last year of
secondary school and the first year of university studies would be provided.
Following completion of the library extension, an additional 5,000 books,



- 11 -
selected to support the development of scientific and technology studies, and
short courses, would be provided. Similarly, the science faculty building,
which contains laboratories for mathematics, physics, chemistry, biology and
geology, would undergo minor repairs and each subject area would receive a
stock of basic laboratory equipment together with the required consumables to
allow demonstration by the instructors and experimental work by the students.
Book and equipment lists have been prepared and criteria to guide the selec-
tion of relevant basic lavel texts and equipment have been agreed with the
iniversity authorities.
3.16      The credit would finances  (a) minor civil works and building
renovation; (b) furniture and laboratory equipment; (c) 7500 library books;
(d) 24 manmonths of overseas training in library management; and (e)
selected related incremental operating costs. Annex 3-6 provides details.
2.   Improved Sector Management for More Effective Use of Resources
3.17      To improve sector management for a more effective use of resources
in the Ministry of National Education (MEN) and higher education, the project
wouldt (a) strengthen MEN's capacity to plan, manage and control personnel
and financial and physical resources; (b) improve MEN's implementation
capacity; (c) support the development of the National Scholarship Service;
and (d) strengthen the planning capacity at the University of Bangui.
3.18      Personnel management.  The project would support the application to
MEN, of the on-going Public Administration Reform, with the twofold objective
of reducing the non-teaching wage element of educational costs and increasing
efficiency. This component is already underway with the completion in April
1987 of a staffing and organization plan for MEN and an indicative plan for
MESRES (as a guide to set up its organization chart and prepare its budget
request for 1988). Measures to control the growth of the wage element would
include: (a) a reduction of the proportion of non-teachers in MEN's staff,
from 112 to 72 over the project life through redeployment into the classroom
so as to decrease the number of students per teacher; (b) a gradual elimina-
tion of staff identified as redundant or unsatisfactory; and (c) strict
application of all existing personnel rules.
3.19      Measures to increase efficiency would includes  (a) a revision of
procedures to further decentralize non-policy decision-making power; (b) a
reduction of staff turnover to 102 p.a.; (c) creation of a management and
staff information system; and (d) strengthening of DAP and a revision of the
systet. of staff evaluation carried out by school inspectors to increase its
objectivity and allow closer monitoring of performance. On the incentives
side, a capacity would be created in DAP to identify the training needs of
staff, design and arrange for appropriate training programs (which would
later be taught with bilateral assistance at the Public Administration
Institute); in addition, the "concours professionell for teachers' promotion
would be reinstated in 1991. Personnel files would be reorganized and
computerized to produce, with the assistance of the data processing services,
statistics on educational personnel. Annex 3-7 gives details on this
component, and Annex 3-1 indicates how it will be monitored at the joint
annual review.



- 12 -
3.20      The credit would finance:  (a) furniture and equipment (including 2
micro-computers) and office supplies for DAP; (b) six manmonths of specialist
services in personnel management, training program design and computer use
and a total of 27 manmonths of training abroad and six manmonths oi local
training in the same disciplines; and (c) selected incremental operating
costs for the new personnel management system.
3.21      Resource Planning and Management.  First, the financial and budget-
ary procedures of the Gen-ral Directorate of Administration and Finance
(DGAF) would be reinforced through: (a) computerization of budgeting,
expenditure and cost control systems to allow regular monitoring of the
budget and the use of funds; and (b) improved procurement, purchasing and
contract management procedures, to reduce costs through larger purchases and
simpler specifications. Second, to build up MEN's capacity to manage
existing infrastructure through increased regional and community resources
and responsibility, the DGAF (jointly with the decentralized DCES) would
maintain a computerized inventory of existing facilities and prepare the
rolling triennial plan for school construction, rehabilitation and mainten-
ance (para. 3.6). Third, to coordinate the collection, processing and
analysis of statistical, financial and personnel information for the educa-
tion sector, the data processing unit, created in May 1987 with assistance
from the Second Technical Assistance project (IDA Cr. 1581-CA), would be
strengthened and a development plan for the gradual computerization of the
MEN would be prepared.
3.22      The credit would finance:  (a) renovation of existing offices;
(b) furniture and equipment of facilities for the DGAF and the data proces-
sing unit; (c) eight manmonths of specialist services to set up the budgetary
procedures, management information and statistics processing system, and
standardize bidding documents; (d) 10 manmonths of fellowships for manage-
ment, procurement procedures and statistics collection and processing;
(e) local training in education statistics; and (f) incremental operating
costs (excluding local salaries). Annex 3-8 details this component.
3.23      Proiect Coordination Unit.  To ensure an efficient, well coordin-
ated implementation of project activities, the Directorate of Projects and
External Relations (DPER) would be rationalized, while a small Project
Coordinating Unit staffed with nationals and attached to MEN's Secretary
General, would have responsibility for all project coordination. This unit
would reinforce the implementation capacity of MEN and would be headed by a
full-time project director, with responsibility for supervision and
coordination of project activities; it would support all directorates in the
area of procurement, financial administration, documentation, monitoring and
reporting. "he director would be assisted by a full-time deputy (TA), an
administrator, and an accountant. To ensure adequate standards with account-
ing, auditing and reporting, the project would provide the necessary consult-
ing services to assist the project management team establish accounting,
monitoring and reporting systems.
3.24      The credit would finance:  (a) the renovation of the facilities
where the unit would be located; (b) offlce furniture and equipment; (c) 88
manmonths of technical assistance in project management, procurement, ac-
counting, auditing and future project preparation; (d) training abroad in
project management; and (e) selected incremental operating costs on a



- 13 -
declining basis. At negotiations, thie Government discussed and agreed with
IDA, the organizational structure, responsibilities and functions of the
project coordination unit and confirmed that procedures for the appointment
of the Project Director and the recruitment of the project management staff
had been initiated. Annex 3-9 details this component.
3.25      National Scholarship Senrice.  The development and reinforcement of
the recently created National Scholarship Service will be supported to allow
it to: first, complete the review of the scholarship rolls started in early
1987; second, revise and prepare coherent and rationalized administrative
texts governing the award and continuation of scholarships; third, traverse
the transitional period between the existing system of scholarship award and
the new system to be based on a quota allocation by subject area; and
finally, undertake key studies to guide the future development of the
scholarship system (ensuring that it reflects the budgetary situation of the
country, responds to the labor market situation (para 2.4) and proposes
criteria to ensure equitable allocation). The scholarship quota system will
contain elements designed to redress the balance between: (a) humanities and
science based subjects; (b) post-graduate and undergraduate level studies;
and (c) studies abroad or inside the CAR; and will propose criteria to assist
disadvantaged groups in higher education inscitutions (women and poor rural
families).
3.26      The project will finance: (a) minor building repairs; (b) office
equipment and furniture; (c) 16 manmonths of local training; (d) 6 manmonths
of specialist services to undertake a study on the education/labor market
relationship; (e) 9 manmonths of foreign and 77 manmonths of local specialist
services in microcomputing and systems analysis to develop the revised quota
system and assist in studies to elaborate quota criteria; and (f) selected
incremental operating costs. Annex 3-6 details this component.
3.27      University Planning Unit.  The university has a small planning unit
to assist in the development and planning of its activities in future years.
The priority task for the planning unit is, on the basis of provisional
estimates of higher level manpower needs of the economy in both quantitative
and qualitative terms, to identify new training fields needing to be
developed, either by adaptation of existing courses, or by the creation of
flexible and rapidly modifiable courses. Given the small size of the CAR
labor market at higher manpower levels, innovative solutions such as
temporary training during a limited number of years, cooperation with
neighboring countries, in-service and sandwich training courses will need to
be examined. The planning unit will cooperate with: (a) the office of
Academic Affairs of the Rectorate, which holds data on student admissions,
progressions and examination results; (b) the National Scholarship Service,
responsible for the operation of the quota system and the award of
scholarships; and (c) the Civil Service Ministry.
3.28      The credit will finances  (a) a micro-computer and related office
equipment; (b) 13 manmonths of local consultants together with regional study
visits; and (c) selected related incremental operating expenditures. Annex
3-6 provides details.

B.   Proiect Cost and Financing
3.29      The total project cost over the six years of implementation is
estimated at US$20.7 million equivalent (net of taxes and duties) with a
foreign exchange component of US$11.9 million or 57%. Base cost estimates
are at March 1987 prices. The project would be financed by IDA (US$18.0
million equivalent or 87% of total cost), the United Nations Development
Program (UNDP) (US$0.1 million equivalent or 12), parental contributions from
the APEs (US$1.2 million equivalent or 6%), and budgetary allocations from
the Government (US$1.3 million or 62). The UNDP would finance volunteer
specialist services for the URCES. The APEs would finance, either in kind
(labor and local building materials) or through cash contributions, the
completion of the classroom structure with locally made blocks. The Govern-
ment would finance a proportion (302) of the incremental operating costs and
the totality of the primary school directors' housing. The major assumptions
in arriving at the cost estimates, as well as the detailed project costs by
component, by cost category and by source of finance are given in Annex 3-10.
3.30      Recurrent cost implications.  Contrary to many social investments,
this project is aimed at improving the overall operation of the education
system with all additional operating expenditures being more than compensated
by saving measures also included in the project. The recurrent coats
generated by the project would be relatively modest in the early years of
implementation but would grow rapidly in the latter years. These would
attain an estimated maximum of CFAF 956 million (constant 1987 prices) after
project completion in 1995, equivalent to 102 of the FY87 education budget.
By that year, the 117 additional classrooms included in the project would be
in full use and pedagogical materials and other qualitative inputs also
provided under the project would require replacement. Of these costs, 661
would constitute the salaries of teachers needed to operate new classes in
the added classrooms, reduce class-size and compensate teachers in the
"concours professionnel' (these incremental salaries have been estimated to
increase from CFAF 78 million in FY89 to CFAF 320 million in FY92 and CFAF
631 million in FY95). The rest of the costs would include the completion of
the in-service teacher training program, the coordination of the inspectorate
services and preventive building maintenance. During project implementation,
the credit would finance on a declining basis (starting at 90Z and gradually
decreasing to 50%) selected incremental recurrent costs generated by the
project, other than the above mentioned teacher salaries. These expenditures
averaging about CFAF 70 million per year during project implementation, would
consist mainly of fuel and vehicle maintenance costs, with limited office
equipment maintenance, consumables and communication costs. The sector
saving resulting from measures included in the project (the review of
scholarship lists, reduction of scholarship expenditures, staff redeployment
and rationalization, reduced rehabilitation and building maintenance costs,
ani improved financial management) are likely to exceed project operating
cost during implementation, and subsequent recurrent cost that the project
would generate in any fiscal year. Reductions in student repetition would
not directly affect the recurrent costs but would reduce the unit costs per
graduate. In 1995, the savings resulting from the project are likely to be
at least 2.2 times the incremental recurrent costs generated and the revised
internal budgetary structure for the education sector, included in the Action
Plan, would have increased material and operating allocations (books and
vehicle operating costs) in real terms by 1452 allowing the Government budget



- I5 -
to assume the responsibility of the recurrent cost of project activities
(Annexes 3-11 and 3-16).
C.   Project Implementation
1.   Preparation. Management and Institution Building
3.31      Status of preparation.  Implementation plans and schedules have
been prepared for each component and are given in the Annexes. Designs for
the civil works have been developed; the primary school building prototype
has been completed and was reviewed at neLotiations. Suitable existing sites
have been identified for all construction activities and the survey for
school rehabilitation and construction has been completed. Furniture and
equipment lists have been reviewed by IDA and found acceptable. A full-time
National Project Director has been appointed and the recruitment of key
technical assistance staff, including the Deputy Project Director, has been
initiated. Prior to negotiations the Government discussed with IDA the
revised administrative texts detailing changes in MEN (School Construction,
External Relations and Projects Directorates). The basic principles of the
Action Plan were agreed upon during appraisal and at negotiationst the
Government gave the assurances listed in para. 3.1.
3.32      Implementation responsibility and schedule.  Implementation details
of each component are given in Annex 3-1 through Annex 3-9 with a sumary
implementation schedule in Annex 3-12. Implementation responsibility for
each component rests with the relevant Ministry Directorate. Coordination of
all components would be entrusted to the Project Coordination Unit# which
would support the directorates in the areas of procurement, financial admin-
istration, documentation, monitoring, reporting and preparation of the annual
review, and act as a liaison with IDA for all technical matters. All project
components have been phased both over time and geographically by staggering
the actions envisaged. Given the absorptive capacity limits, the innovative
nature of some of the project elements, and the regional profile for the
sector, the implementation period has been cautiously planned over a six-year
period, with a disbursement lag of six months, plus six months for the
closing of the accounts. Assurances were received that the project would be
Implemented along the arrangements and schedules agreed upon during
preparation.
3.33      Specialist services and training.  The project would provide a
total of 141 years of specialist services (78% local staff) and a total of
55 years of training (97% locally). Terms of reference were agreed during
preparation, and the recruitment of key technical assistance staff has been
initiated; the signature of these contracts would be a condition of the
credit effectiveness. For training abroad funded by the credit, assurances
were given during negotiations, that: (a) such training would be supported
on the basis of mutually agreed selection criteria for programs and
candidates; and (b) recipients of long-term training abroad would remain in
their assignments for at least three years following the completion of their
programs. Annexes 3-13 and Annex 3-14 give details.



- 16 -
2.   Procurement, Disbursements and Special Account
3.34      Procurement. Importeu equipment, major civil works contracts and
goods, including the printing of textbooks (totaling US$6.72 million equiva-
lent) representing about 57% of the total value of civil works and goods,
would be procured through international corlpetitive bidding (ICB) it, accord-
ance with IDA's guidelines and grouped, te' the extent practicable to facili-
tate competition in bidding, into bid packages of at least US$250,000 equi-
valent. Where ICB pro.edures are used a preference of 7.5% of the total bid
price for domestic contractors, and 15X or the applicable customs duty
whichever is the less, for domestic manufacturers, would be giver in accord-
ance with IDA guidelines. Other contracts which are snall In size or involve
widely dispersed sites, not likely to attract foreign bidders, would be
awarded through local competitive bidding (LCB) or local shoppings Procure-
ment through LCB, in accordance with procedures acceptable to IDA, would be
applied to bid packages valued at between US$20,000 and US$250,000 equivalent
and would comprise civil works contracts and goods (totaling US$2.65 million
equivalent). Procurement through local shoppina, in accordance with local
procedures would be applied to small contracts for civil works and goods
valued at less than US$20,000 equivalent (totaling US$190,000 equivalent).
Local construction materials and unskilled labor services (totaling
US$2.15 million equivalent) represent community and Government inputs not
financed by IDA. Specialist services would be procured in accordance with
IDA's guidelines on the use of consultants. (These, together with overseas
training, are estimated to cost US$5.0 million equivalent). Bidding packages
(announcements, bid documents and proposed contract awArds) estimated at more
than US$250,000 equivalent, representing about 57% of the total value of IDA-
financed contracts, would be subject to IDA's prior review. Accordingly, the
remaining 43% of contracts, would be subject to: (i) post review (for
contracts estimated at between US$20,000 and US$250,000 equivalent); or
(ii) random review (for contracts estimated at less than US$20,000 equiva-
lent). During negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it will
apply the procurement procedures and arrangements outlined above. Procure-
ment arrangements are summarized in the table following and detailed in
Annex 3-15.

The project is expected to be completed by June 30, 1994. Annex 3-16
summarizes Government and Parents' Associations contributions to the project,
and Annex 3-17 gives the disbursement profile.
3.36      Spec(al Account.  To facilitate disbursements, US$400,000 equiva-
lent, denominated in CFAF, would be advanced from the IDA credit and
deposited in a Special Account held in a local commercial bank and operated
jointly by at least two of the following: the Project Director, the Deputy
Director and Secretary-General. The amount is estimated to cover about three
months' expenses for all project expenditures. Replenishment applications to
IDA would be aggregated into packages of at least US$100,000 equivalent and
be fully documented. Expenses related to contracts and/or expenditures
valued at less than US$20,000 equlvalent would be reimbursed against
certified statements of expenditu:es (SOEs), for which documentation would be
retained at the Project Unit for review by IDA supervision missions and for
regular semi-annual audits. Reimbursement applications would also include a
statement of account movements since the last application, with the balance
certified by the bank holding the account, and a reconciliation sh'wing that
the balance represents the original amount less payments awaiting
reimbursement or small working advances. The commercial bank holding the
Special Account would also provide additional services in the form of
expenses classification services. Should any disbursements made subsequently
be disallowed by IDA, the Government would redeposit the corresponding amount
tc the Special Account. Given the availability of the Special Account, the
minimum application for direct payments would normally be for the equivalent
of US$20,000.



- 19 -
3.   Accounting. Auditing and Reporting
3.37      The Project Director would maintain separate accounts for the IDA
credit and Government counterpart financing. These Accounts would be further
subdivided into expenditures incurred for each component. At negotiations,
the Government gave assurances that: (a) all project accounts will be
audited annually and all disbursements against SOEs will be audited semi-
annually by an independent auditor, acceptable to IDA; (b) the Government
will submit to IDA the audit reports by December 31 of each year and the
audit reports on SOEs by June 30 and December 31 of each year beginning in
1988; (c) the audit reports will certify that the funds were used for the
purposes for which they were provided; and (d) the Project Unit will submit:
(i) semi-annual reports to IDA on project implementation and expenditures by
June 30 and December 31 of each year beginning in 1988; (ii) a summary report
of the Action Plan each year in September; (iii) special reports on a need
basis; and (iv) a completion report within six months of the IDA credit
closing date.
IV. PROJECT BENEFITS AND RISKS
4.1       Benefits.  The first benefit of the project has already been
achieved; its preparation was the occasion of a positive dialogue with the
Government, a detailed diagnosis of the education sector, followed by the
elaboration of a strategy of rehabilitation, which is pragmatic, coherent and
adapted to the country's need. The second benefit is educative; the project
will promote improved quality of inputs, input mix and resource management,
resulting in more efficient operation, reduced costs/graduate, and increased
pupil achievement. At the primary level, it is expected that for an increase
in costs per student of 152, these improvements would translate into a reduc-
tion of the dropout and repeater rates by as much as 30X, and a 402 decline
in the costs per graduate. The third benefit is both educative and economic;
the project will place a brake on post-secondary education and ensure a
closer qualitative and quantitative match between the output of the educa-
tion/training system and the needs of the economy. The fourth benefit is
economic and institutional; a more efficient allocation of resources, com-
bined with better management, will reinforce, at the sector level, the
Government's structural adjustment program.
4.2       Risks.  The project has two main risks.  First, the CAR has no
experience of complex sectoral project in the field of education. To ensure
that the sector budgetary targets set out in the Action Plan are acted upon,
the Ministry of Economy, Finance and Planning, would participate in the
annual review process. In addition, implementation problems arising from the
complexity of the project are addressed by staggering the actions envisaged
for both institutional developr-ent and the physical rehabilitation of
schools. Second, at present the CAR education sector suffers from weak
financial management, planning and budgeting, and ongoing projects encounter
delays in the provision of counterpart funds. The project has been designed
to alleviate this situation with a carefully planned approach of Government
to sector rehabilitation; by the increased participation of local communities
witb labor and local imputs; by short-term consultancy services in financial
management; and by the training program for the national team in project
management.



- 20 -
V. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
5.1       Prior to negotiations the Government: (a) discussed and agreed with
IDA on the organizational structure, responsibilities and functions of the
Project Coordinating Unit, and the revised administrative texts reorganizing
MEN (DCES, DPRE, and DEPP) (para. 3.24 and 3.31); (b) proposed satisfactory
candidates for the full-time Project Director (para. 3.31); (c) discussed
with IDA a review of the experience with the classroom prototypes and the
construction methodology in rural and urban areas (para. 3.31); and (d)
submitted to IDA a short-list of consultants for technical assistance
contracts, and an agreement in principle from the volunteer organizations to
participate in the rehabilitation program (para. 3.33 and 3.8).
5.2       During negotiations the Government and IDA discussed and agreed on
the general composition of a three-year rolling Action Plan, which would be
examined at a joint annual review, and which contains annual interim targets
aimed during the life of the project (1988-1994), at: (i) monitoring the
proportion of the Government recurrent budget allocated to the education
sector; (ii) reducing Government expenditure on scholarships from 16% to 8%
of the education sector budget, and focusing it on agreed-upon key
scientific, technological and managerial fields; (iii) raising expenditures
on primary education from 52% to at least 60%, and on miaterials from 2% to at
least 8% of the education recurrent budget; and (iv) reducing the proportion
of non-teachers in total education staff from 11 to 7X through staff
redeployment by June 30, 1993; and (v) following-up the implementation of
regulations on repeaters (paras. 3.1 and 3.31).
5.3       During negotiations the Government gave assurances that it will:
(a) incorporate, in the annual budget law, the sector budgetary targets
agreed with IDA during the annual joint review and monitor their
implementation and relationship to the overall public investment
and expenditure programs under the SAL (para. 3.31);
(b) during the joint annual review, discuss and agree with IDA, on the
annual in-service training plan; the annual classroom
rehabilitation program; the personnel program and primary education
enrollment and class-size targets (paras. 3.5, 3.10, and 3.18);
(c) by the 1989 review, discuss with IDA the school maintenance
procedures and guidelines, and introduce them following approval
(not later than September 30, 1989) and thereafter cause the
primary inspectorates to provide adequate regular support and
supervision (para. 3.12);
(d) award all project training, funded by the credit, according to the
agreed criteria and procedures, and ensure that the beneficiaries
work for the sector for three years thereafter (para. 3.33); and
(e) implement the project along the arrangements and schedules agreed
upon during preparation, including the procurement procedures, and
the arrangements for accounting, auditing, and reporting as agreed
(paras. 3.32, 3.34 and 3.37).



- 21 -
5.4       As a condition of effectiveness, the Government would have major
technical assistance and consultant contracts signed (the managing consultant
for textbooks, regional coordinators -- volunteers -- for classroom
rehabilitation, the Deputy Director and the short-term procurement specialist
for the PCU (para. 3.33)
5.5       Continued disbursement of the credit would be conditional upon
agreement each year between the Government and IDA on the contents of the
Action Plan and satisfactory completion of the joint annual review of
progress made in implementing this action plan (para. 3.1).

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
INTERNAL EFFICIENCY, QUALITY AND STUDENT ACHIEVEMENT
A.   Internal Efficiency and Cost Per Graduate
1.        Calculation of the cost per graduate is linked to the internal
efficiency of the system. This is unfortunately very poor, the education
system in CAR being characterized by extremely high repetition and dropout
rates. These rates do not reveal any significant changes over the past 6
years, and it may consequently be assumed that, in the absence of a policy
change or qualitative improvements, the repetition and dropout rates will
remain fairly stable.
2.        Taking 1,000 CI entrants in 1975, and applying the repetition rates
observed each year for each level to obtain the cohort reconstruction from
the theoretical flow, the total of CEPE graduates is 210. an efficiency rate
of 21Z. Of the original 1,000 entrants; 553 have dropped out along the way,
having attended school for an average of three years; 447 of them have gone
as far as CM2, and of these, 53Z have failed the CEPE. Altogether, the
cohort has received 4,974 years of instruction, an average of 24 school years
per graduate. This latter figure is important, since it enables the calcula-
tion of the cost per graduate and the comparison of the CAR's performance
with that of other countries in the region (23 in Benin, 20 in Senegal, 18 in
Niger, 15 in Burkina Faso). Average performances within the region are
clearly poor, and that of CAR one of the poorest.
3.        A student who completes primary school, whether he graduates or
not, spends an average of eight years there (instead of six), which implies
that each student repeats twice. This explains why CM2 students are gener-
ally between 13 and 15 years of age, instead of 11, which would be the norm.
Generally speaking, progress through the school system is slow: the average
age of students taking the BAC is over 20, and students who go on to higher
education spend a long time at that level, up to age 30 or more. Quite
understandably, they regard the scholarship they receive as a wage, viewing
the status of student as a substitute for employment.
4.        Internal performances at the secondary level, both in the first
(BEPC preparatory) cycle and in the second (BAC preparatory) cycle, are
hardly any better than those noted at the p:imary level. Using the same
survival rate method, it takes 15 student-years (instead of the theoretical
four) to produce a BEPC graduate, and 23 student-years to produce a bachelier
(instead of the theoretical three). This latter figure is explained more by



- 38 -
ANNEX 2-5
Page 2 of 5
the very high rate of BAC failure (almost 90Z) than by the number of
repeaters and dropouts. I/
S.        Based on these data, it is thus possible to estimate costs per
graduate, multiplying the cost per student-year by the number of student-
years necessary to the production of the graduate concerned.
Table 1 - Cost per Graduate (1985)
------------------- (CFAF) ------------------
Number of                                         Cumulative
Student-Years    Unit Cost per    Unit Cost per  Unit Cost/
per Graduate   Student-Year (1)   Graduate (1)  per Graduate
CEPE                  24              15,000             360,000        360,000
BEPC                  15              31,000             465,000        835,000
BAC                   23              31,000             713,000     1,538,000
Technical BAC         32              98,000          3,136,000      3,961,000
(1)  Borne by CAR.
In the case of the general BAC, the total cost per bachelier is CFAF
1,538,000, and in the case of the technical BAC, 3,961,000 (twice as high).
These figures represent, respectively, 13 and 36 times the country's GNP per
capita.  For the general BAC, this is three times the annual cost of a
student of higher education in Bangui, and 1.5 times the annual cost of a
student abroad.  By comparison with public-sector wages, these costs are
equivalent to 1.5 times the average annual salary of a civil servant.
6.        With respect to primary and general secondary education, it seems
unreasonable to try to bring down the costs per student-year, given that unit
costs consist almost entirely of teacher salaries and that the teacher-pupil
ratio is already outside the limits of sound pedagogical practice. On the
contrary, steps should be taken to improve internal efficiency, thereby in
fact incurring additional expenditures. At the primary level, the distribu-
tion of textbooks and teaching supplies could push up the cost per student-
year by CFAF 1,500-2,000, an 8-12Z unit cost increase. These measures should
have a significant impact on performance, in terms both of lowered repetition
rates and of higher exam pass rates. It is true that it is difficult to
foresee the extent of such improvements, and there is little literature on
this point. However, the distribution of textbooks in the Philippines had a
significant impact on student performance, and it has now been established
(cf. Psacharopoulos and Woodhall, 1985) that the supply of textbooks to
1/   The repetition and dropout rates used here are average rates for the
latest years recorded (36? BEPC pass rate for the six school years
1980-85, 12.52 BAC pass rate for the four years 1981-85). They produce
slightly different results from those of the ORIVEL/RASERA report for
February 1986, since they are based on more recent data.



- 39 -
ANNEX 2-5
Page 3 of 5
schools that have practically nothing is one of the most cost-efficient
measures that can be taken.
7.        Tables 2 and 3 simulate the effect on the cost per graduate both of
lower repetition and dropout rates and of a higher CEPE pass rate,
consequences of the qualitative improvements in primary education resulting
from measures introduced by the project. In both cases, a modest change
(102) is sufficient to bring down the cost per graduate by 3Z for each
effect, and on the order of 6Z if the two effects are simultaneous. In the
hypothesis that, following the improvements made, the cumulative repetition
and dropout rates will go down by 301, dropping from their present 42% record
to a more reasonable 291, and that the CEPE pass rate will go up by 30%,
rising from its present level of 47% to 611, the reduction in the cost per
graduate would be 24.7% and 17.4%, respectively, while the two effects
together would lead to a reduction of over 40% in the cost per graduate. For
a given budget, this would mean a 40% increase in the number of new places
available for children presently excluded from enrollment.

8.        In the secondary system, the restoration of minimal selection
criteria will result irnitially in an automatic increase in unit costs per
student, since the e1imination of clandestine enrollments will reduce the
number of students per class without altering the volume of expenditure at
this level. There again, an increase in cost per student-year will not raise
the cost per bachelier, but very probably bring it down. Clandestine
enrollees are those who failed the sixth grade entrance exam, and, with very
few exceptions, are less competent than those who passed. In view of the
poor internal efficiency of the secondary system (38 student-years per
bachelier, 13% BAC pass rate), only one sixth grade entrant out of 15 passes
the BAC, and it is most likely that he will come from the group of
"competent" students. Consequently, elimination of the incompetent students,
by reducing the number of students per class (often over 100), will enable
the system to function better, and lead to better performances by the
"competent" students, thus reducing the cost per bachelier at the same time.
B.   Educational Quality and Achievement
9.        Table 4 summarizes the primary and secondary examination results
over the last seven years. Despite an increase of almost 25% in the total
primary enrollments between 1980 and 1985, the number of students in the
final year of primary school has increased only slightly and consequently the
number of CEPE examination candidates have remained almost constant. The
pass rate for this examination, and that for entry into the first year of
secondary schooling, has remained relatively constant with less than 50X
succeeding at the CEPE and about 252 of all candidates gaining entry to
secondary school. With an increase in the total enrollments (and hence in
the number of student-years of instruction given each year) but a constant
number of successful graduates from the primary system, there has been a
decline in efficiency with a drop in educational quality at this level of
education.



- 41 -
ANNEX 2-5
Page 5 of 5
10.        The situation at the secondary level is different, nevertheless the
result is the same.  The rapid expansion of secondary enrollments at both the
lower and upper cycles has led to an increasing number of candidates
presenting for examination at the levels of BEPC and Baccalaureate. Apart
from an initial rise in the actual number of successful candidates at the
beginning of the period, the success rate at both of these examinations has
fallen faster than the increase in the number of candidates with the results
that there were fewer successful candidates in 1984-85 than in 1980-81. Once
again, with increased enrollments but reduced numbers of successful
graduates, the efficiency of the system has declined and a drop in
educational quality may be deduced.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
RETURNS TO EDUCATION
A. Wage Estimates
1.        The method of calculation used is the classic, simplified method,
in which the private rate of return rp is equal to:
r  -         pay at level s            -           Bay at level s-1
loss of earnings (number of years of schooling between
s-I and s multiplied by pay at level s-1)
and the social rate of return is equal to:
r8        same pay factor as for rp
same denominator as for r plus public cost of schooling to

B. Analysis of Rates of Return.
2.        Social rates of return are traditionally lower than private rates
of return because of inclusion In the denominator of the schooling cost
borne by the State. This is born out in the result slhown below in Table 2:
Table 2

tunity cost                    9.0          13.4           5.6        23.3 a/
Primary without
opportunity cost           n.a. b/       n.a. b/          14.6        50.0 a/

secondary                     31.9           6.9          23.7         4.9
