a/   For the calculation of the social rate of return in the formal private
sector, the reference income taken for primary education is the
notional income of a farm worker (estimated at 6,500 CFAF) and not
that of illiterates In the informal private sector.
b/   The value tends to infinity as the denominator is zero.
3.        An analysis of these rates reveals that in CAR, as in almost
every other African country or in other developing regions, it is prinlary
education that produces the highest rates of return, except for the social
rate of return in the public sector. This is explained by the fact that
the civil service almost always requires the BEPC as its minimum entry
level, CEPE holders having practically no choice other than precarious,
low-paid jobs (temporary laborer, day laborer, etc.). Apart from this
anomoly, it appears that wage scales in the civil service are more
compressed than in the private sector. There is strong convergence toward
the mean, which explains the very low social rate of return of higher
education (3.2%). The reason why students continue to apply for higher
education is that they do not have to pay for it, a situation implying poor
allocation of funds within the education system. Generally speaking, the
rates given above are overestimated, In the sense that they assume that the
students have not repeated a "post-obligatory" school year (i.e. a year for
which a loss of earnings can be recorded), and that they will not be
exposed to periods of unemployment.



- 45 -
ANNEX 2-7
Page 3 of 3
4.        The impact of these different factors is shown in Table 3, which
is based on the most interesting rate in CAR's present situation, namely
the social rate of return in the formal private sector. As will be seen,
with agricultural income as the reference income, the rate of return to
primary education is 23.3% with the opportunity cost taken into account,
and 50% without this opportunity cost. In reality, the opportunity cost is
probably positive, since non-enrolled children are frequently engaged in
farm work, but it is very probably lower than the average income of the
adult worker, which means that the true rate of return for primary
education is within the 23.5%-50% range.
5.        With respect to the effects of repetitions and of periods of
post-schooling unemployment on the rates of return, they obviously cause
them to decline, more sharply in cases of repetition (which produce a
double cost in terms of loss of earnings and schooling) than in cases of
unemployment, which only generate loss of income. These effects, which may
be added together, are interesting in the sense that they heavily
underscore the basic argument of the present project. Repetitions and
unemployment have only a slight impact on the rate of return to primary
education (because of the small loss of income and the moderate costs of
schooling at this level). Even with five repetitions, or three repetitions
followed by two years of unemployment, primary education retains an
enviable rate of 15.4%. However, the rates for the first cycle of
secondary school, already showing a poor performance, fall rapidly to
insignificant levels. The rates of return for the BAC and the university
diplomas fare somewhat better, but their average level is still lower than
primary education (6.7% and 7.8% instead of 15.4%) and, taking account of
errors of measurement, these investments can no longer be regarded as
socio-economically justified.

3.         Description of CAR's Primary Teacher Training Program.  The heter-
ogeneous nature of the primary teaching corps is explained in part by the
gradual evolution and development of the teacher training system, and in part
by the examination performance of the trainees upon completion of their
courses and their recruitment into the teaching profession. Between 1962 and
1971, primary teachers were trained in Bangui as Agents Superieur or Institu-
teurs Adjoint with one year of studies.. From 1970 until 1985, the Teachers
Training College (Ecole Normale d'Instituteurs - ENI) at Bangui trained
Instituteurs in a three-year program, and between 1980 and 1985, a UNESCO
program trained Instituteurs Adjoint with a two-year training course. In
1985-86 CAR rationalized its teacher training program into a single
structure. This program, based at the ENI in Bambari, which requires comple-
tion of secondary schooling (baccalaureate) plus a psychopedagogic test as
the minimum entry requirements, lasts for two years, one year of basic
training and one year of practical/theoretical training combined, and leads
to an examination and one-month test in the classroom. Successful graduates
are awarded the "Certificate d'aptitude pedagogique A lenseignement
fondamentalv (CAPEF) and have the rank of Instituteur.



ANNEX 2-8
- 49 -                              Page 4 of 5
Unsuccessful candidates are allowed to retry their examinations twice and, if
they do not succeed, they are granted the rank of Instituteurs-adjoint.
Although the CAPEF is a primary teaching qualification, a number of those
holding this certificate are employed in secondary education or adminis-
trative duties. However, it is important to note that there has been no
regular in-service training of teachers and no internal professional
advancement of teaching staff through competitive examinations since 1983.
4.        Primary Teacher Training Capacity.  Following the rationalization
of teacher training institutions and 5eginning in the academic year 1986-
1987, the ENI located at Bambari, which opened in 1983-84, offered the only
pre-service training for primary teachers in the CAR. The college in Bangui
was transformod into a National Center for Continuing Education Upgrading
(Centre National de Formation Continue - CNFC) and coordinates the regional
retraining capacity in the seven Regional Pedagogical Centers (Centre Peda-
gogique Regionaux - CPR). The ENI at Bambari is currently underutilized and
its first graduating class of 1985-86 totalled 33 students of whom 25 were
successful. With a reorganized academic year of 200 days, taking into
account students on practical teaching exercises in local schools, and an 80%
utilization of the existing buildings, its output of teachers could be sub-
stantially increased to about 200 graduates p.a. from w;he existing two-year
program. Other teacher training capacity at the CNFC and the CPR's will be
used for in-service training and upgrading of existing teachers, although it
would be possible to operate pre-service courses in these institutions should
the need arise.

5.        Demand for Primary Teachers.  Table 5 throws some light on the
magnitude of the demand for primary school teachers in CAR until 1993.  This

different hypotheses concerning (a) enrollment growth; and (b) changes in the
pupil-teacher ratio. All calculations include an annual teacher retirement/
wastage rate of 2%. This is close to the observed loss of teachers from the
profession in the last two years but would increase in outer years with the
gradual aging of the teaching corps. The first three rows of Table 5 show
the requirements for new tea.chers assuming a pupil-teacher ratio constant at
the 1985/86 value of 65:1, while the last three rows assume a gradual decline
in the pupil-teacher ratio from 65:1 in 1985/86 to 61:1 in 1992/93. The
three different levels of enrollment growth illustrate, respectively, the

enrollment ratio constant at its 1985/86 level of 79%; and (e) 4Z which would
allow a gradual increase in the primary enrollment rate to 86% by 1993.

6.        Balance between demand and supply.  A comparison between the
existing in-service teacher training capacity and the demand for new primary
school teachers shows that:
(a)  if annual enrollment growth were to slow down as low as 2%, there
would be ample capacity to produce new teachers in the numbers
required; this would allow a gradual reduction of the pupil-
teacher ratio;
(b) if enrollments were to grow at 3% p.a., the ENI could meet a sub-
stantial, although declining share of new teacher requirements.
The same conclusion would hold true under a 4% growth assumption,
but the resulting gap would be larger. The gap could be filled
without creating additional teacher training capacity, through a
package of actions combining: (i) reassignment of non-teaching
staff into the classroom; (ii) recruitment, from other sources and
at levels below that of instituteur, for instance, among university
graduates following accelerated training at CNFC; (iii) extension
of the number of hours taught by teachers. The action plan to be
agreed upon each year between the Government and the Bank would
include monitorable criteria for objective (i) as of PYI, and for
objectives (ii) and (iii) as of PY2.
In conclusion, the above shows that the existing pre-service training
capacity for primary school teachers, combined with measures to rationalize
the use of teaching/personnel, will be sufficient to meet the country's
needs for such staff during the period considered. Growth rate during
1980-86 was 0.75%, but this is well below the long-term trend, which is
closer to the 2.5% projected for 1985-1990.

Page 1 of 7
CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
FINANCIAL IMPLICATIONS OF EDUCATIONAL POLICIES
1.        Background.  During the preparation and appraisal of the current
project, the education sector dialogue between the Bank Group and the
Central African Republic included a detailed examination of the financial
implications of alternative policies aimed at improving quality in primary
education. In March 1987, IDA organized a seminar in Bamako for Ministry
of Education Officials from both Mali and the Central African Republic, to
review alternative policies related to primary education, with the help of
an education simulation model developed by the Bank Group. Discussions
centered around the effects on primary enrollments, qualitative efficiency
and unit costs per primary graduate of different policies of: (a) public
sector budget growth; (b) changes in resource allocation between the educa-
tion sector and other sectors; (c) resource allocation within the education
sector and hence between different levels of education; and (d) resource
use designed to change the unit costs of primary education.
2.        Initial data, prepared with assistance of the Bank Group, was
used to identify a base case which reflected the situation in 1985/86 and
1986/87. The base data was examined and refined by the Government team and
agreement reached on the current performance of the education system. The
simulation model was then used to analyze the effect on primary school
enrollment of changes in a wide variety of parameters starting from the
agreed base case. The model allowed changes in the following groups of
parameters: financial/budgetary; teacher inputs; internal performance; and
other pedagogical variables. A summary of the key parameters is given
below:
Financial/budgetary
1.   Annual rate of growth of the Government's recurrent budget;
2.   Share of above devoted to the education sector;
3.   Annual rates of growth for key sub-sectors of the education
system:
(a) higher education (except scholarships)
(b) scholarships
(c) secondary education (general)
(d) primary teacher training
(e) secondary education (technical)
(f) central administration
4.   The proportion of compensation for annual inflation included in
annual salary adjustments; and



- 52 -
ANNEX 2-9
Page 2 of 7
5.   Rate of growth of fixed costs.
Teacher Inputs
6.   Number of hours per week taught by teachers (primary)
7.   Proportion of primary teachers entering the teaching force at the
different grades of:
(a) instituteurs
(b) instituteurs adjoint
(c) agent superieur and others
8.   Proportion of primary teachers changing scale (due to promotion)
at flxed points on the salary scale
9.   The cost of administration per primary teacher.
Internal Performance
10. The repetition and dropout rates at each grade level of the
primary system.
Pedagogical Parameters
11. Cost of materials per student
12. Number of hours of instruction per week received by students
13. Number of pupils per class
3.        Assumptions Behind Simulations.  The share of the country's
resources devoted to the education sector is governed by parameters (1) and
(2); while resource allocation between the different levels and types of
education is controlled by the parameters contained under (3). The general
wages policy in relation to inflation is given in (4). Resource use in
primary education and its effects on the student unit costs are derived
from the remaining parameters. The model applies the budgetary and finan-
cial parameters to its initial values to calculate the residual expenditure
available for primary education. The remaining parameters which measure
the cost of materials per student, the cost of administration per teacher,
and the evolution of the teacher salary costs according to qualifications
and the progression of teachers on the salary scale, are used to calculate
the unit cost per student. The total recurrent budget resources for
primary education are divided by the per student unit costs to arrive at
the primary student enrollment possible with the resources and parameter
values defined in the model. The model also calculates other outputs such
as the primary enrollment ratio, the average number of years of instruction
per graduate, the new entrants into primary schooling, the pupil-teacher
ratio, the teacher requirements, and building investment costs.
4.        Results of Simulations.  Simulations were carried out to analyze
the scope for improving the internal efficiency of primary education while
maintaining the current primary enrollment ratio. The simulations, which



- 53 -
ANNEX 2-9
Page 3 of 7
were aimed at a more effective and efficient use of educational resources,
were made using the educational financing model and were analyzed in two
separate groups. First, the effect of individual changes in the policy
variables on the unit costs/student, unit costs/graduate and total enroll-
ments was examined to determine the sensitivity of the system to individual
parameters and to identify the policy variables with the most leverage.
Second, once the above had been completed, a series of changes in groups of
related policy variables were examined for their cumulative effect on the
system. Table 1 summarizes the results of the changes in the individual
policy variables and shows: (a) changes in the resource levels and the
distribution of resources between different types of education (Simulations
2 and 3); (b) changes in the renumeration and workload of teachers
(Simulations 4 and 5); and changes designed to improve educational quality
and internal efficiency (Simulations 6 through 10).
5.        Simulation 1 of Table 1, which should be considered as the base
case with no policy modifications, shows that if no parameter changes are
made, and if the annual growth rates of the sub-sector budgets have values
equal to the overall growth of the Government's budget, then, not only does
the primary enrollment ratio decline to unacceptable levels, but absolute
primary enrollment decreases. As a result, it is necessary to examine
policy changes which are likely to increase enrollment and hence maintain
the primary enrollment ratio at an acceptable level. Simulations to
examine the effects of a higher rate of growth in the Government's recur-
rent budget are not realistic given the current economic situation. (The
growth parameters for the total Government recurrent expenditure budget are
taken from the IMF April 1987 projections and held constant throughout this
exercise.) Simulation 2 shows the effect of a 10% increase in the
proportion of these resources being allocated to the education sector.
While this simulation manages to increase primary enrollment, the primary
enrollment ratio declines.
6.        Simulation 3 shows that even when all sub-sector budgets except
primary education are frozen, enrollments increase but the primary
enrollment ratio falls by 15%. Hence, it may be concluded that the
possibility of increased resources for education and changes in the
internal distribution of those resources do not prevent a decline in the
primary enrollment ratio, and the way to prevent a decrease in enrollment
and to maintain the enrollment ratio at an acceptable level is to reduce
unit costs per student.
7.        Unit costs per student are composed of three separate elements:
the teaching salary cost, the administrative cost and the materials cost.
The salary cost normally increases automatically as teachers progress
through the civil service pay scales. Simulation 4 shows that a salary
freeze is a powerful policy change and the unit cost/student is very
sensitive to the decline in the real value of the salaries. Another
related measure which reduces unit costs is the "loading" of teachers.  In
Simulation 5, the number of hours taught by teachers is increased by 10%
from its current value of 23.1 hours/week and the number of hours of
instruction received by each class group is reduced to 24.3 hours/week with
a resulting fall in the unit cost/student. Actions to reduce either the
administrative or materials components of the unit cost per student run
contrary to the objectives of the project (improvement of the quality and



- 54 -
ANNEX 2- 9
Page 4 of 7
internal efficiency of primary education). In fact, it seems unreasonable
to bring down the costs per student-year; on the contrary, steps should be
taken to) improve internal efficiency, thereby incurring additional
expenditure. Ttems likely to have a positive effect on the internal
efficiency, include increased pedagogical materials and supplies and a
strengthened and more dynamic school inspectorate. Simulation 8 therefore
indicates the effect of an increase in the cost of administration per
teacher due to an improvement in the activities of the school inspectorates
and their support of primary teachers, and the result of a substantial
increase in the availability of pedagogical materials and supplies. Both
of these qualitative improvements incur a rise in the unit costs per
student. To reinforce the qualitative improvements, to support teachers
and to improve internal efficiency, a reduction in class size is modelled
in Simulation 9 and, as a consequence of all qualitative inputs, the
assumed resulting reduction in internal repetition is simulated in
variation 10.
8.        In Table 2 the individual changes are combined to examine the
cumulative effects of a series of policy changes and the results of four
specific sets of changes. As before, Simulation 1 repeats the base case
values of all parameters with no policy changes. Simulation 2 of Table 2
combines the majority of policy changes needed to bring about a substantial
reduction in the unit costs/student. The changes include: limiting the
growth of sub-sector budgets so that budgetary increases are allocated to
primary education; increasing the hours taught by teachers and reducing the
amount of instruction received per class group to a level of 27 hours per
week; and freezing salaries. The results show that unit costs are
stabilized and primary enrollment more than doubled by the end of the
fifteen-year period. Simulation 3 includes all the above changes plus
additions designed to revitalize the teaching profession: an increase in
supervision and support for teachers with a more dynamic and active primary
inspectorate; the revival of professional exams and advancement for
teachers; and the recruitment of new teachers in a more balanced proportion
between different qualifications; increased expenditure on pedagogical
materials and supplies, and a slight reduction in the average class size.
These qualitative improvements allow the reduction in the repetition rates
of about 40%. Simulation 3 shows an increase in the unit costs/student and
as a consequence, slower growth of total primary enrollments, but more
importantly, a substantial decline in the unit costs per graduate. This
final simulation corrects the results previously obtained by unfreezing the
sub-sector budgets and allowing limited subsector budgetary growth. The
growth of secondary technical education expenditure at a rate above the
norm reflects the increased expenditure resulting from the current AfDB
financed technical and vocational education project. Overall, the compo-
sition of student unit costs has been modified in favor of educational
inputs, the unit costs/graduate has been halved, enrollment has increased
by half as much again and the primary gross enrollment ratio has been
maintained close to its previous high level.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
THE GOVERNMENT'S EDUCATIONAL STRATEGY
A.   Background
1.        In April 1982, the CAR Government organized a national seminar to
conduct an evaluation of twenty years of post independence educational
experience. On the positive side, it was found that the system had
achieved fairly high primary enrollment ratios (95% for boys, 61% for
girls) and had met its objective of training a cadre of high level adminis-
trators as required for the development of a new nation. However, these
achievements were outweighed by the negative aspects. First, the CAR's
education system, which had been geared to the training of civil servants,
had fueled rural migration and contributed to a disregard for productive
activities, especially agriculture. The system lacked effective orienta-
tion and screening mechanisms, so that most students, regardless of their
individual abilities, were channelled toward the more prestigious, longer
types of training, and the less demanding literary or human sciences
streams (80% in humanities at the baccalaureate level) at the expense of
shorter, more practical types of training and of mathematic and scientific
disciplines. As a result, by 1982 with the civil service saturated, the
nation had large numbers of unemployed generalists, at the same time as a
critical shortage of middle level technicians and managers and even good
skilled workers. Second, the broad coverage in primary education had been
achieved at the expense of quality, which had been left to deteriorate, to
wit excessive cla.s sizes, ill-adapted programs, underqualified teachers --
especially in scientific disciplines -- grossly insufficient operating
allocations, dilapidated facilities and an inefficient administration. The
resulting high repetition rates, poor examination results, and increase in
the number of pupil years required to produce a primary graduate (from 12
in 1971 to 24 in 1986), had led in turn to high costs per graduate, low
labor productivity, a mediocre level of student achievement at all levels
of the system, and graduates' difficulty in finding modern sector jobs.
B.   The 1984 Reform Proposals
2.        The reforms which were proposed to correct this situation
(Ordinance No. 86-031 dated May 14, 1984) aimed at strengthening and
reorienting the educatior. system to adapt it to the needs of the country
and the abilities of individual students, so that each would be equipped to
contribute more fully to the country's socio-economic development. The
central objectives of the reform included:
(a) to extend from six to nine years (5 + 4) the basic education
cycle;
(b) to reduce regional disparities in primary enrollment ratios;



- 59 -
ANNEX 2-10
Page 2 of 4
(c) to increase quality in primary education through a revision of
curricula, introduction of Sangho, the national language, as a
medium of instruction, and of productive work; strengthening the
supply of pedagogic materials and teachers' pre- and in-service
training, rehabilitating facilities;
(d) to diversify the second cycle of basic education into three
streams: general, technical and agricultural; and
(e) for all other levels, to revise curricula.
3.        Considerable attention was devoted to the underlying philo-
sophical principles of the Reform, i.e., developing in the student popula-
tion a sense of creativity, responsibility and private initiatives, as well
as a commitment to hard work. A new teacher profile -- characterized by a
spirit of pedagogic innovation, practical knowledge of the environment and
strong community leadership -- was defined, and a new teacher pre-service
training program was developed. However, there was no indication of how
the Reform would be operationalized; no consideration had been given to its
costs and financial implications and to the linkage with employment and it
soon became clear that the proposed Reform was too broad and too ambitious.
There was a need to: (i) develop a coherent, global view of the sector,
especially with respect to student flows and budgetary allocations;
(ii) prioritize, especially between qualitative and quantitative objectives
and between levels of education; and (iii) formulate for the sector as a
whole, a long-term development plan consisting of well articulated sub-
sectoral categories; each of these should cover policy meaRures, training,
investment and recurrent resource requirements to allow the gradual lifting
of the various constraints -- human, institutional and financial -- in
order to reach the objectives set under (ii). In particular, Government
began to realize tlpt any attempt to revise the contents of the curricula
would be doomed to failure as long as the delivery system (pedagogic
support and control by the inspectors) would remain inoperative.
C.   A More Realistic, Intermediate Strategy
4.        By 1986, Government had recognized that the objectives of the
Reform were long-term in nature and would require a broad, sustained effort
from all the parties involved. For the medium-term, it decided to focus on
rehabilitating the existing system, prior to introducing changes of sub-
stance or to expanding. This rehabilitation approach centered on:
(a) reinforcing quality, especially at the primary level;
(b) strengthening the management and the administration of the sector
through a decentralization process; and
(c) mobilizing the support of all the parties involved -- teachers,
parents and students.
5.        To achieve the quality objective (a), the Government is prepared
to accept a temporary stagnation -- or even a modest decline -- in the
primary enrollment ratio (from 782 in 1987 to 74% in 1993), with expansion
limited to underserved areas and to what is needed to keep up with



- 60 -
ANNEX 2-10
Page 3 of 4
population growth (2.8%). Budgetary (and other) resources will be
concentrated, by level of education, on primary education, and by type of
expenditures, on materials to optimize the utilization of teachers. An
initial increase in unit costs (from 15,000 CFAF to 16,500 CFAF) is
proposed to allow an injection of pedagogic material and to finance the
upgrading of teachers' skills, in turn leading to a reduction of unit costs
per graduate. New curricula are being prepared, but the emphasis will
continue to be on the 3Rs, with the introduction of Sangho temporarily held
up, and productive work only tested on an experimental basis. The new
programs wil' be introduced successively, by grade, starting in PY3, once
inspectors have been retrained, pedagogic materials are ready to be
delivered and a first wave of teachers has received special training to
teach that grade.
6.        The cecentralization process proposed to reach objective (b)
started with the issuance of a new MEN organization chart (Decree No.
86-016 of January 21, 1986), which clearly separated pedagogic from
administrative functions and underlined the Cabinet's role in policy and
strategic matters. Under this new institutional set-up, authority in
routine managemetnt matters will be increasingly delegated from the Cabinet
to directors and ministerial instructions will be transmitted to the field
through a network of inspectorates, five regional academic inspectorates
and 19 primary inspectorates at the district level. Personnel management
(including administrative and inspection ratings) is being tightened in
line with the Public Administration Reform, inspectors will be trained in
administration and management, both at the district and the school levels,
and they will be provided logistical means.
7.        Finally, the need to develop a partnership between local communi-
ties, MEN personnel in the administration and at the institution level and
students (i.e., objective (c)) is dictated not only by financial, but also
by social considerations. In the present context of fiscal austerity, an
increase in the total government recurrent budget or in the already high
(24%) share going to education is very unlikely, hence the need to raise
additional resources in the form of contributions from parents' associa-
tions (APEs). Beyond this, however, there is a need to convince parents
that the ultimate beneficiaries of a dynamic and relevant education system
are their children, whose future is at stake. The Ministry has therefore
engaged in a sensitization campaign to mobilize local communities and APEs,
arguing that self-help, careful management of such scarce resources as
school facilities or textbooks, as well as a responsible financial
behavior, are the best wzys for the CAR education sector to regain credi-
bility and attract external support from bilateral and multilateral
sources. New administrative arrangements are being put into place at the
school level to reflect these new roles. However, in order not to
jeopardize educational development in areas of lesser parental concern and
support, Government will continue to finance the basic civil works and
materials package necessary for minimal operating conditions while the
community will provide the supplementary items.
8.        All the objectives set out for other levels of the sector reflect
the same spirit of rehabilitation and consolidation, self-help, discipline
and rigor and have been articulated into a "schema de coherence". They
include, for secondary education, increased selectivity and the



- 61 -
ANNEX 2-10
Page 4 of 4
strengthening of teachers' skills, especially in scientific disciplines;
for higher education, a reduction in student numbers, improved orientation
towards the most needed disciplines through the use of scholarships and
quotas, and a new emphasis on short, technical training streams to respond
flexibly to evolving labor market needs. In an effort to alleviate the
burden of MEN, a new Ministry of Higher Education and Scientific Research
was created in 1986, to oversee the university sector, while vocational
training -- also in the process of being reorganized -- was placed under
the authority of the Ministry of Civil Service and Labor. This entails a
risk of fragmentation of the sectoral management perspective, and will
require new coordination mechanisms, which are being developed. Finally,
Government plans to formulate, in the years ahead, detailed subsectoral
strategies for secondary, higher, vocational/technical education which will
be submitted to the donors at UNDP Round Tables.
9.        The above strategy will take courage and determination to be
implemented, and results may be less than spectacular. However, the
Government realizes that the country has no other option, given the crisis
situation in the sector and the scarcity of national resources and aid
flows, but to restore efficient operating conditions in order to achieve
more within the present constraints, and to subsequently attract external
resources when conditions are ripe to move to the next phase. In the
meantime, the realism and clear sense of purpose of the Minister, the high
quality and commitment of the Minister's Cabinet, and their reliance on a
systematic, carefully planned approach, are major assets for the successful
outcome of this long-term endeavor.

ANNEX 3-1
Page 1 of 7
CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
ACTION PLAN AND ANNUAL REVIEW PROGRAM
1.        Background.  The budgetary situation in the CAR deteriorated
rapidly during the 1970s and the early 1980s, mainly because of a severe
imbalance between the level of public revenues (which were limited) and the
public sector recruitment/employment policy (which guaranteed employment
for graduates, thereby growing much faster than the revenue). Since 1982,
the Government's economic policy has attempted to correct the situation,
but in order to re-establish economic growth, certain basic policies will
have to be pursued for some years to come. These include: (a) a reduction
of the budget deficit; (b) an increase in public sector non-salary expendi-
tures to allow minimum efficient operations; and (c) reduction of the
proportion of public expenditures in the GDP to stimulate savings and
productive investments. The CAR has adopted a policy of gradually reducing
public sector recurrent expenditures with a view ;.o increasing investments
(today, close to zero) and reducing debt service. This policy has as its
consequence, to reduce the proportion of public sector recurrent
expenditures in GDP from 12% in 1986 to 9.5% in 1991. The GDP is projected
to grow at a little over 8% p.a. in current prices, and the inflation rate
is estimated as 4.5% during the same period. Because of the projected fall
in the proportion of public sector recurrent expenditures in GDP, the
recurrent budget is expected to evolve from 36.9 billion CFAF in 1987 to
43.6 billion CFAF in 1993 (current prices). This nominal increase of 18%
corresponds in reality to a fall of 9.2% in constant prices.
2.        Objectives.  This project, aimed at improving the quality aid
efficiency of schooling, especially at the primary level, would support the
implementation of the Government's education policy in the context of its
structural adjustment program. To ensure that sufficient resources are
available for the qualitative improvements which are the project's key
objectives and that these resources are optimally allocated, the Government
and IDA would discuss and agree upon a three-year rolling Action Plan to be
monitored each year at a joint Government/IDA review. The Action Plan
would consist of, sector budgetary targets; the scholarship program and
budget; of staffing and pedagogical targets; and the monitoring of project
implication (specifically: the in-service training plan, the annual
classroom rehabilitation and maintenance program, and the personal plan).
As a three-year rolling plan, it would provide detailed values of the
quantitative and qualitative performance indicators together with specific
actions for the first year, and it would outline the indicative targets and
a work program for the remaining two years of the period. Details of the
contents of the Action Plan, are provided in the Table 1.

The education sector budget is dispersed over six different chapters of the
Government budget. However, when all the relevant expenditures are added
together, the total educational expenditure in 1985 was 25.4% of Government
recurrent expenditures. The distribution of sector expenditures, in
percentage terms, by level of education and by nature of the expense havre
been projected over the six years of active project implementation and are
attached in Table 2. These targets are to provide guidance to the inter-
mediate stages in the Action Plan and are subject to revision during
implementation.
3.        Mechanism.  While substantial improvements in the quality and
efficiency of the education process are the major objective of the project,
changes in the internal budgetary allocation of the sector and educational
expenditures are seen as esserIcial means of achieving this objective. As a
result, financial and budgetary aspects of the project are emphasized in
the project conditionality. Given the importance attached to this condi-
tionality, and the seriousness of the consequences of the non-respect of
such conditionality (the possible suspension of credit disbursements), the
Action Plan would be monitored annually, throughout the life of the project
at joint meetings of the Government and IDA. The objective of the annual
review would be to monitor the progress made during the previous year on
the key indicators defined at prior reviews and to agree on the details of
a program for the continued disbursement of the credit during the following
year. To accomplish this task, the review process would: (a) examine
critical annual performance indicators, as defined and quantified at the
previous review; (b) agree upon the targets to be adopted for the following
year, the means of achieving these targets, and the quantitative indicators
that will be monitored at the next annual review; (c) list the indicators,
together with their indicative values, for the remaining two years of the
rolling three-year period; (d) agree upon the content of the investment
program; and (e) examine the potential use of credit funds, inside the
existing project description, which become available as project
implementation proceeds.
4.        Administration and Management.  The review committee of the
Government will be a small group of government officers from the policy and
decision levels of the following Ministries and departments: (a) Economy
and Finance; (b) National Education; (c) University of Bangui; (d) National
Scholarship Service; and (e) Plan. Each Ministry or department will be
represented at the decision or policy level by one member. Given the
importance of financial issues contained in the review, its scope (which
spreads over the two education ministries and the scholarship service
located in the Presidency), the chairmanship of the review committee will
be with the Ministry of Economy and Finance. The review committee will
meet at least four times per year: once before the review in order to
examine the review documentation and prepare for the joint meeting; once
with IDA to undertake the annual review; and once after the joint meeting
to finalize any adjustments required for the year to follow. The
secretariat of the review committee will be assured by the Project Director
and the Assistant Director of the Project Coordination Unit. The review
committee will not have the responsibility for overseeing the general or
day-to-day management of the project, but will limit its role to the
monitoring of the global project conditionality. Full responsibility for
the regular implementation of the project rests with the Project



- 66 -                     ANNEX 3-1
Page 5 of 7
Coordination Unit and, through it, with the individual directorates
implementing the various project components.
5.        ReRorting and Documentation.  The secretariat of the review com-
mittee will prepare the necessary documentation for the joint annual review
between IDA and Government. This documentation will be short, concise and
aggregate, while covering the total education sector. The report will
focus on the three main areas described in Table 1 (budgetary targets and
real expenditures; the scholarship program, the quotas based on subject
orientation and the scholarship budget and expenditure; and staffing and
pedagogical targets). It should contain two major sections: (i) a brief
historical analysis on the performance during the previous 12 months; and
(ii) detailed proposals of the objectives and means to be adopted for the
next 12 months, together with global proposals for the remaining two years
of the three-year rolling Action Plan. It should not exceed four pages in
length and, where possible, should be presented in tabular form. An
outline of the draft Action Plan and its presentational framework is
provided in Table 3. This will be completed with the indicative values of
the critical indicators at negotiations.
6.        Means and Costs.  The operating costs of the Government review
committee will be extremely limited. The project will provide indemnities
for the five members of the review committee; all other inputs, such as the
preparation of the annual review documentation and incidental expenses
related to the joint annual review and the operation of the review secre-
tariat, are funded by the project under the PCU component.

Table 3 - Indicative Contents of the Draft Action Plans
1.   Sector Budgetary Targets:
(a) Monitor the proportions of the total Government recurrent budget
allocated to the education sector to ensure that an appropriate
allocation satisfactory to the Association is programmed for each
year of the project;
(b) Raise the proportion of the education sector recurrent budget
allocated to primary education;
(c) Raise the proportion of the education sector recurrent budget
allocated on pedagogical materials and supplies;
(d) Reduce the proportion of the education sector recurrent budget
allocated on scholarships;
(e) Monitor the education sector investment budget.
2.   Scholarships.  Taking into account the reduced allocation resulting
from (1.d) above; propose an annual breakdown of the total number of
scholarships by:
(a) subject grouping (Sciences and Humanities);
(b) level of studies (lst, 2rd, or 3rd levels);
(c) location of studies (France, Other foreign, CAR)
The breakdown should indicate both the number and percentage of total
scholarships broken down by the above categories.
3.   In-Service Training Program.  The action plan should update and revise
the schedule of the training provided in Table 3 of Annex 3-2. In addi-
tion, the reduction of the average primary school class size should be
programmed to take into account the planned reduction from 62 to 55 by
1993/94.
4.   Personnal Plan.  The proportion of non-teachers in the education
sector personnel should be reduced from 11% to 7% by 1993/94; this will be
achieved through the redeployment of administrative staff to teaching
duties (predominantly at secondary education). In addition, new recruit-
ment should take place to keep pace with the planned expansion of the
primary enrollments. The annual programming of the above should be
presented as follows:
(a) Transfer to the classroom: Surveillanc General, others.
(b) Leaving service: (i) Retirements; (ii) Expatriates end of
conttact; (iii) Elemination of personnel permanently studying;
(iv) Medical reasons; (v) Transfer to the Bangue de lersonnel.
(c) Recruitement: (i) Staigaires contractuels; (ii) Others
5.   School Rehabilitation and Maintenance Program:  The action plan should
update and revise the physical implementation plan contained on pages 17,
18 and 19 of Annex 3-4.

Page 1 of 16
CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUPPORT FOR THE RETRAINING OF THE PRIMARY TEACHING FORCE
1.        Background.  Although only about 62% of CAR's primary teachers
are considered qualified by national standards (i.e. having a rank of
instituteur-adjoint or instituteur), for lack of resources in 1980 all in-
service training activities were discontinued, resulting in a degradation
of the basic and professional skills of teaching personnel. While the
qualification profile of the teaching corps improved during the 1975-85
period, the standards were somewhat relaxed to meet a fast growing demand
for teachers. In addition, due to insufficient supervision and frozen
salaries, teacher motivation, professionalism and status, have dropped,
leading to absenteeism, lack of innovation and research In classroom
practices and loose monitoring of student achievement. Such a deteriora-
tion is all the more worrisome that the 1984 Education Reform defined a new
teacher profile which calls for a leadership role and a more active partic-
ipation of teachers in the development process.
2.        This trend has had a negative impact on the quality of education,
in turn carrying a high social and financial cost in terms of resources
spent on students who dropout, graduates who are not readily employable and
the low efficiency of the civil service. To reverse this deterioration,
the Government is initiating a threefold strategy. First, it proposes to
strengthen teacher pre-service training and to reinstate in-service
training. To that end, in 1986, one of the country's two primary teacher
training colleges (Bangui) was transformed into a National Center for
continued upgrading (Centre National de Formation Continue - CNFC), the
apex of a network of 7 regional pedagogic centers (Centres pedagogiques
regionaux - CPR). Second, the role of inspectorates as the Ministry's
field representatives will be reinforced. To that effect, a special
program was started at CNFC with the assistance of the French Fonds d'aide
et de cooperation (FAC) to retrain, over a three-year period, CAR's five
academic inspectors, 36 primary inspectors (out of 50) and the 70 pedagogic
advisors. Those primary inspectors and the pedagogic advisors based at the
CPRs will in turn conduct in-service training for school directors and
teachers. Those in charge of school inspection will also provide better
pedagogic control and support, thanks to an increased availability of
logistical means. Third, it is proposed to sequentially regain control of
the educational process in each of the six grades of the primary cycle
(grades I and II in 1988 and 1989, III and IV in 1990 and 1991, V and VI in
1992 and 1993) through the combined effect of grade-specific teacher
retraining and an increased supply of (FAC financed) pedagogic material.
3.        Obiectives.  In support of the above rehabilitation strategy,
this component aims principally at raising the quality of existing
teachers, a prerequisite to a successful implementation of the Reform. It
would also provide a cost-effective alternative to the 'instituteurs only'
option, to be examined when the Government conducts its forthcoming



- 70 -                          ANNEX 3-2
Page 2 of 16
regrading exercise of the whole civil service in 1988. Shorter term
objectives are:
(i) in light of the recent redefinition of teachers' profile and
role, to evaluate experience with, and the contents of, the pre-
service teacher training program established in 1984 and make
proposals for its improvement, to be implemented by the start of
the 1988/89 school year;
(ii) to reinforce the quality of pedagogic control, supervision and
support provided to the primary schools by the inspectorates;
(iii) to improve primary school timetables and management practices at
the primary school level through an in-service training program
reaching about 845 school directors (about 84X of the total);
(iv) to reinstate a regular in-service program to upgrade the basic
and professional skills of about 965 experienced but under-
qualified agents superieurs and agents, and 1,733 instituteurs or
institutours-adjoints, i.e. respectively 54 and 622 of these
categories;
(v) to motivate teachers to continuously upgrade their skills; and
(vi) during PY1, to develop concrete proposals to imiprove efficiency
in a number of problem areas such as teachers' career stream and
a more intensive use of human and physical resources.
4.        Required outcomes.  Coupled with textbook provision, this
component is expected to have a synergetic impact on thz effectiveness of
the teaching process. This will (i) raise internal efficiency, thereby
reducing unit costs per graduate by at least 6.5% and as much as 42Z (under
the combined effect of a reduction in dropout/repeater rates and improved
final pass rates); (ii) deepen skills acquisition by students, thereby
improving external efficiency since graduates will be more readily employ-
able or trainable (at present new graduates frequently remain unemployed
for 1-3 years because of the lack of employment possibilities); (iii) equip
the teachers to play the more active role expected of them under the
Reform; (iv) improve the delivery of educational services, and through an
optimised primary school timetable promote a gradual reduction in the
average class-size, thus increasing the chances of success of any
subsequent curricula changes; (v) reduce the need to resort to the more
expensive 'instituteurs"; and (vi) improve staff morale, since in-service
training would be linked to encouraging professional advancement
opportunities (cf. para 8).
5.        Strategy.  This component will take as a starting point the
recommendations of a series of recent MEN seminars on the implications of
the 1984 Reform. This Reform, rhich aims at increasing the relevance of
the education system to the local environment, calls for teachers to take
more initiative and exercise community development leadership. In
actuality, it has largely remained a dead letter because its implementation
would require individuals with stronger basic skills and more practical
experience and a more efficient planning and management system. The



- 71 -                          ANNEX 3-2
Page 3 of 16
project will approach these sets of constraints in a systematic manner. As
shown in Table 1 at the end of this Annex, during a first phase (PY1 and
2), it will support a series of workshops, seminars and task forces for key
personnel involved in the management of primary teacher training, the
delivery of educational services and primary school operation. These
activities will: (i) help to adapt pre-service training to the require-
ments of the Reform; (ii) emphasize efficient management and administration
at the school level and prepare the inspectors and pedagogic advisors to
conduct in-service training at the CPRs (this sub-component to include
scholarships abroad for 2 and a study tour for 3); and (iii) lead to
concrete proposals on matters requiring further technical analysis or a
political consensus, e.g., confirmation procedure, the status and training
needs of practice school staff, changes in the school calendar, etc.
6.        The second phase (PY2-PY5) will consist of the in-service
training program itself (details in Table 2). Each year, for each CPR, the
program will include one three-week session for school directors and 3
quarterly modular sessions for primary school teachers (eleven weeks for
agents, superieurs/agents and seven weeks for instituteurs/instituteurs-
adjoints). The subjects covered will echo key themes of the project such
as organization and the school timetable, personnel management, school
maintenance, use and care of textbooks. For directors, the emphasis would
be on the management of financial, human and physical resources. For
teachers, the training sessions would include a remedial program in basic
disciplines, a refresher course in pedagogic techniques, classroom practice
and grade specific approaches.
7.        Although the Program will be offered eventually at the 7 CPRs
(plus CNFC, in Bangui), it would be started on a staggered basis, beginning
with the regions of Project concentration (Bambari in Ouaka, Bangassou in
Mbomou# Bangui-ville and CNFC) in PY2 (school year 1988-89). At the end of
this first experimental year, an evaluation would be conducted at CNFC and
the program would be revised as needed before being extended to two more
regions in PY3 (Berberati and Bouar, serving the western part of the
country) and two more in PY4 (Bossangoa and Kaga Bandoro, serving the
northern part). Although CPRs have a nom.nal capacity of 50, to ensure
quality the size of the groups would be limited to a maximum of 25 the
first year, and of 35 subsequently. The national in-service training
program and background documentation, as well as general principles
governing implementation, were developed during preparation by CNFC, under
the supervision of DRPFE. However, each CPR would have a measure of
freedom to adapt the program to the local circumstances and environment and
to produce its own complementary background documentation. By June 30 each
year every regional individual program, including detailed plans for
logistical organization (size and profile of the groups, precise arrange-
ments for the replacement of participants, payment of per diem, transport)
would be submitted for approval, together with a detailed budget, to a CPR
coordinating unit to be created at CNFC. In turn, by July 30 each year,
CNFC would submit to IDA for review a proposal for the national program.
The CPRs would receive equipment and furniture as needed. Tables 3 and 4
provide details on and a summary of the numbers retrained.
8.        To encourage the teaching staff to undertake in-service training,
the project would help Government to reinstate the "concours professionnel"



- 72 -                          ANNEX 3-2
Page 4 of 16
(professional competitive exam) in 1991, which would allow 15 teachei p.a.
to be upgraded by one level. Prerequisites for taking the exam would be at
least 5 years teaching experience, satisfactory inspection ratings for the
previous three years and successful completion of one in-service training
course in che five previous years. Because of these conditions and because
the resulting salary bill implications will have to oe agreed with the IMF
(whose program presently includes a freeze on promotion-related salary
increases) the first competitive exam is not expected to take place until
1990. This question will therefore be examined at the mid-term review.
9.        In support of on-site training, and for all teachers interested
in continued upgrading of their skills, three types of activities would be
undertaken.  First, a radio in-service training program would be set up.
The program, to follow closely the CPR in-service curriculum, would be
developed in 1988 by INRAP's Educational Radio and TV Department, with
technical assistance. Broadcasting would start in October 1988, at the
same time as the CPR training cycle. Every month, six half-hour periods
would be aired for a teacher audience, plus one for directors and one for
inspectors/pedagogic advisors. Each program would be repeated once, to
reach a maximum number of listeners. Second, feedback to the CPRs would be
encouraged and discussed at Regional workshops (Journees pedagogiques
regionales, 2 days/year) which had been discontinued in the early eighties
but would be revived in the three regions of project focus (Ouaka, Mbomou
and Bangui-ville). The results of these sessions would be summarized in
each CPR's quarterly Pedagogic Liaison bulletin, and sent to CNFC for
distribution to the whole CPR network. Third, a correspondence in-service
training system would be tested in Ouaka, which is one of the project areas
and has a large pool of pedagogic resources (CPR + ENI Bambari). The
program would be based on a monthly correspondence liaison between the CPR
and individual teachers participating on a voluntary basis. It will be
evaluated at Bambari's CPR after one year and, if warranted, will be
gradually extended on the same geographically phased basis as the in-
service training program. IDA will provide a small budget for the initial
experiment (and subsequently will assist Government in mobilizing financial
support from other donors for its extension).
10.       Finally, the whole pre- and in-service training program would be
evaluated at mid-course (PY4) with the assistance of independent
specialists in order to: (i) draw the lessons of experience; (ii) revise
the program as needed; and (iii) define a mechanism to turn it into a
permanent f2ature of the CAR education system. This will include securing
new sources of funding for the post-project period.
11.       Coordination and management.  Responsibility for coordinating and
managing the above tasks will rest with the Directorate for Pedagogic
Research and Teacher Training (Direction de la Recherche Pedagogique et de
la Formation des Enseignants - DRPFE) through a Technical Co-mission for
the planning and programming of pre- and in-service training (Commission
Technique de Planification et de Programmation de la Formation Initiale et
Continue - CTPF). This Commiss'on, consisting of high level personnel from
the Ministry of Education, especially the General Directorate of Education
(Direction Generale des Enseignements), the General Inspectorate, INRAP,
ENI, and CNFC, was created during project preparation to define the
objectives and contents of the program, which will be presented at a



- 73 -                     ANNEX 3-2
Page 5 of 16
national trainers seminar (summer 1987). Key members of the Commi4sion
will be selected to form two task forces which will be enlarged on an ad
hoc basis by national specialists and short-term consultants to submit
proposals for the revision of pre-service training, and for improvements in
teacher career stream and educational resource use. The national training
program will be prepared by CNFC, INRAP, and LNI staff, then adapted and
delivered by CPR inspectors and pedagogic advisors. A CPR coordination
unit will be created in CNFC to provide guidance and support to the CPRs,
exercise quality control at the beginning of each school year, dissMninate
and analyze the results of each regional program and ensure liaisol between
DRPFE and the field.
12.       Costs.  The detailed item costs given in the DATGEN haz served
as a basis to calculate an average cost per man/day for both staff and
participants. Differences between the various activities reflect the
number of staff involved, duration, locatior of activity, and the use of
various types of staff (civil servants, local consultants, international
consultants).

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
DEVELOPMENT OF TEXTBOOK AND PEDAGOGIC PUBLISHING AND DISTRIBUTION PROGRAM
1.        Background.  The Government supplies, free of charge to primary
schools, a small number of pupil textbooks (French and mathematics) and a
small number of teachers' guides (French, mathemathics, geography,
grammar). The number supplied is not adequate, with book-pupil ratios
varying from one French book for 3-4 pupils in the final year of primary in
Bangui schools to no pupils' books in rural schools, with a national
average of 1 book per 10-20 pupils. To supplement the meagre Government
supply, some local community parents' associations have started to buy
books. However, books currently used, with few exceptions, are unsuitable.
Most were written for the Francophone West African region in general, were
published more than 10 years ago are often ill-adapted or out-dated, and
were published and manufactured abroad and imported into the country. The
cost of books is high, averaging 2,110 CFAF in 1986, and even new books
developed by local authors, are published by foreign publishers using high
production specifications which place the cost of the books beyond the
Government. The Ministry has not attempted to influence the publishers by
rea .3tlng more economical production specifications nor to evaluate the
pedagogic value of the books or the real amount of work in a book which can
be covered in one school year.
2.        Each year the "Commission du choix des livres" produces a tenta-
tive purchase list of titles and quantities based on estimated available
Government funds. The DBCM then invites known suppliers to make an offer
and once the cheapest offer is identified, quantities are reduced to fit
the Government budget with the "Con.nission du choix" deciding the priori-
ties. Books are transported from the coast to Bangui by transport agents
and after customs exoneration are taken to one of the DBCM's warehouses.
The DEPF1 instructs the warehouse chief on the articles to be supplied to
each inspectorate. The allocation is calculated on the basis of infor-
mation in the school inspectors' end of year reports, and when the
inspectors come to Bangui for their annual conference at the end of August
they collect their supplies of books, exercise books and chalk. They must
arrange transportation of the materials themselves. However, sometimes a
parents' association helps, sometimes a local businessman. Delivery from
the inspectorates to the schools is the responsibility of the school
director. Storage facilities and security for books at all levels are
either inadequate or non-existent and a national consciousness of the need
to conserve scarce resources is missing.
3.        Objectives.  This component aims to increase the number of educa-
tionally appropriate books in schools and to ensure their continuing avail-
ability. Approximately 430,000 pupils' and teachers' hool will be bought.
The books' average life span will be increased throup           es in specifi-
cations and improvements to storage.  The educational value of books will



- 86 -
ANNEX 3-3
Page 2 of 11
be improved through strengthening publishing knowledge, evaluation proce-
dures and educational measurement. Specifically the project aims to:
(a)  improve procurement procedures at the DBCM, and use these proce-
dures to purchase a core package of basic books, which would last
3 years, while encouraging publishers to produce cheaper books;
(b)  increase the budgetary allocation for education materials and
supplies so that subsequent pedagogical materials may be
purchased using government resources;
(c)  encourage APEs to purchase textbooks and pedagogical materials in
quantities and subject areas additional to the core package
funded by the Government;
(d)  improve school, inspectorate, and central warehouse storage; and
institute regulations for management of physical resources;
(e)  develop a centre of technical information on publishing, avail-
able to the Ministry, and train local staff in educational
publishing;
(f)  develop and train the DEOS (educational evaluation department)
and study book use and educational achievement; and
(g)  develop book evaluation procedures and improve the pedagogical
value and physical quality of experimental documents.
4.        Required outcomes.  An average of I book per 2 pupils in the
basic subjects; cheaper and more suitable books resulting from communica-
tiorn and co-publication between the Ministry and publishers, and suitable
storage and distribution arrangements for books.
5.        Strategy.  The textbook element of the project covers three main
activities:
(a)  Purchase of books:  bulk purchase by the project through improved
procedures of 3 years' supply of books throughout the country
(See Table 1 for books to be purchased);
(b)  Book distribution and storage:  improvement of book storage
facilities and of their management throughout the distribution
system; and
(c)  Textbook publishing:  improvements in educational books' content
and relevance with strengthened book publishing and evaluating
competencies, educational evaluation and the production unit for
experimental material.
6.        Improved purchasing and procurement procedures would include the
proper evaluation of the educational content and quality of the books being
considered for purchase (para 7 (b)) as a forerunner to the assessment by
the DEPF1 of the quantities of books needed throughout the country and the
deliberations of the existing book selection commission. All books,



- 87 -
ANNEX 3-3
Page 3 of 11
whether from INRAP or from a foreign publisher, would be evaluated in two
phases: (a) pedagogical relevance, content and presentation; and
(b) physical specifications, before being considered for purchase. Quanti-
ties of books needed will be projected and accumulated for several years so
that better discounts can be obtained from publishers. The books to be
purchased by the project are intended to have revised "basic" production
specifications aimed at reducing costs, so that the country can afford to
restock them in the future.
7.        The improvement in the content of books would be brought about
by:
(a) the transformation and strengthening of the existing school book
publishing unit at INRAP into the Ministry of Education textbook
service, as both a source of technical knowledge and advice and a
publishing centre for developing locally written manuscripts;
(b) the creation of a commission, meeting annually, to evaluate the
pedagogical content relevance and presentation of the educational
books available and build up a reference centre for information
on the educational value of books;
(c) the further development of the education evaluation unit in INRAP
so that it can conduct studies of importance to the Government in
arranging its educational priorities; and
(d) the purchase of some new equipment to improve the quality of
typesetting/origination ana printing of experimental texts in the
printing unit at INRAP.
8.        For all three main groups of activities, training and consultancy
services are included. Limited renovation of some existing buildings in
Bangui is provided and, according to needs, renovation of storage facili-
ties in the prefectures, to ensure improvement to the book distribution
system.
9.        Coordination and management.  Several directorates of MEN (INRAP)
are involved in the management of the textbook component. A consultant
supervisor reporting to the Inspector General of Schools and the Secretary
General of MEN is in charge of the management of the component as a whole.
The coordination of roles is shown in Table 2. To permit regular
monitoring and control of this component, annual reports prepared by the
cimponent supervisor affirming the satisfactory development of the
component activities should be submitted to the Government and the
Association.
10.       Costs and Quantities.  Investment costs are shown in the attached
detailed tables.



- 88 -

2.        Cadre institutionnel existant
(a) Au niveau central, la Direction des Constructions et des
gquipements scolaires (DCES) du MEN qui relave de la Direction
Generale de l'Administration et des Finances (DGAF) est charg6e
de tout ce qui concerne la construction, la refection,
l'entretien et l'quipement de l'ensemble des locaux scolaires.
La DCES est notamment responsable: (i) d'administrer, pianifier
et superviser les travaux de construction scolaire; (ii) de
centraliser les informations sur l1'tat du pLtrimoine;
(iii) d'elaborer un programme d'interventions en fonction des
priorites et des moyens disponibles; (iv) de suivre l1'volution
des couts de constructi.n; (v) de mettre en place un rgseau de
services decentralises; (vi) d'elaborer et mettre en oeuvre un
programme de formation des chefs d'etablissement en matiere
d'entretien; et (vii) de gerer les moyens materiels en liaison
avec la DGAF et la Cellule Informatique du Cabinet du Ministre,
notamment par l'informatisation. Jusqu'ici, faute de
responsables possedant suffisamment d'exp6rience, ladite
organisation n'a pas encore pu accomplir sa mission. Tres vite,
le diagnostic etabli a aemontre que la seule solution permettant
dtatteindre ces objectifs consistait a restructurer la DCES et a
entamer une deentralisation;
(b)  Au niveau regional, les Inspections Academiques qui reprgsentent
les services du MEN assurent le bon fonctionnement et le controle
de tous les etablissements relevant de leurs juridictions. En
matiere de constructions et 6quipements scolaires, ces
inspections ne disposent d'aucun moyen, d'oiu la proposition de
les doter de conseillers techniques;
(c) Au niveau des collectivites locales, seules les communes, a la
differeace des villages, des Sous-Prefectures et Prefectures
interviennent en matiere d'etablissements d'anseignement
fondamental;
(d) Au niveau des ecoles, seules les Associations de Parents d'eleves
(APEs) se manifestent parfois activement pour la construction ou
l'entretien des batiments. Par contre le degre tres inegal de
responsabilit6 en matiere de conv-rsation du patrimoine scolaire
a ete relev6 chez les directeurs d'ecole. Le contraste est
saisissant entre ecoles voisines, selon la maniere dont, se
heurtant aux memes difficultes, leurs responsables ont ou non su
mainte-ir le patrimoine qui leur 6tait confie.  D'oi l'extreme
importance attachee a preparer les mattres a leurs
responsabilites, a les y soutenir et encourager leur comportement
en cette matiere;
3.        Financement
(a) Au niveau central, s'il repr6sente le quart du Budget de l'Etat,
celui de i'Education Nationale ne consacre que 4.5% de ses
credits (soit moins de 400 millions de francs CFA) aux frais de
fonctionnement (et pratiquement rien a l'entretien des bgtiments:

en 1987 pres de 1.000 &coles primaires devaient se partager 9

(b) Au niveau r6gional, en l'absence d'une programmation budg6taire
des frais de fonctionnement pour chaque inspection acadgmique,
ces dernieres n'ont aucun moyen de gerer un budget;
(c) Au niveau des collectivitgs locales, seules les communes,
disposant, a la difference des villages, Sous-Prefectures et
Prefectures, d'un budget relativement autonome, peuvent etre
appelees a contribuer financierement aux charges d'entretien des
installations scolaires. Ieurs ressources sont restreintes
depuis q't'en 1982 1'Etat ne leur verse qu'une subvention
forfaitaire, mais elles disposent aussi de ressources propres,
toujours infgrieures au montant de la subvention;
(d) Au niveau des villages, sans personnalit6 financiere, les
villages, n'en representent pas moins un potentiel gconomique:
ils maintiennent le tissu social rural, gardent 1'acc6s aux
ressources naturelles, et accueillent un nombre croissant de
dgSus de l1enseignement et de la ville. On aurait tort de ne pas
penser a eux pour la conservation (voire l'extension) du
patrimoine gducatif. Homologue urbain du village, le quartier
est, en Centre Afrique encore plus disparate sociologiquement que
celui-1a. I1 n'est pourtant pas impossible de lui faire prendre
conscience d'interets communs A promouvoir par un effort
solidaire, comme le montrent, par exemple, certaines realisations
du Developpement Communautaire Urbain (exemple: le quartier BOY
RABE de Bangui);
(e) Au niveau des Associations (APE), les familles, dans leur grande
majorit6, ont de petits moyens financiers qui ne croissent pas.
Toutes sont cependant tenues de contribuer au cout de ltducation
de leurs enfants et d'acquitter A cette fin une redevance
scolaire. Les sommes que representent cette redevance scolaire
sont trop precaires pour qu'on ne les gere pas de facon aussi
economique, sure et transparente que possible. Jusqu'ici les
familles contribuent tres in6galement a la conservation et a
l'entretien du patrimoine scolaire; elles considerent qu'il
appartient a la puissance publique d'y faire face. Afin de
renverser cette tendance preoccupante, le Gouvernement, face a de
serieuses contraintes de ressources, a decide de mobiliser en
faveur de l'enseignement primaire des sources de financement
complementaire et de substitution au budget de l'Etat, deleguant
aux regions et notaument aux APEs le soin de financer en partie
la construction, l'equipement et le materiel p6dagogique par le
biais des contributions (cotisations, taxes, dons ...).
B - Stratzgie propos6e par le projet.  Tenant compte du diagnostic du
contexte actuel et de l'objectif principal qui consiste a rehabiliter
l'environnement des espaces educatifs tout en sauvegardant la valeur des
investissements realises et futurs, le projet prgvoit:

1.        Cadre institutionnel:
(a) la restructuration et le renforcement de le DCES et sa
dgcentralisation au niveau regional;
(b) la creation d'une capacitg technique au sein des inspections
academiques des trois regions du projet par l'adjonction a ces
derniares d'unites regionales des constructions et des
egguipements scolaires (11RCES). Dans le fil de la politique de
r6gionalisation marquee par la creation de cinq Inspections
d'Acadgmie, ces unites (URCES) relevant techniquement de la
Direction des Constructions et des Equipements Scolaires, feront
partie de l'equipe constitu6e autour de chaque Inspecteur
d'Academie et placee sous son autorite. Chargees des quelques
dizaines d'ecoles de leur sectaur geographique, elles devront
tenir l'inventaire detaille des batiments et des equipements, les
visiter regulierement et en surveiller la maintenance. Chaque
annee, elles proposeront, au conseil de chaque Acole, le
programme chiffr6 des travaux d'entretien a realiser et en
discuteront les modalites d'execution sur les ressources locales.
Apres aeoption de ce budget, elles en deviendront mattres
d'oeuvre, coordonnant et controlant l'activite des mattres,
eleves et familles, comme des ent:eprises appelges a intervenir.
Les URCES seront basges, l'une a Bangui, et les autres a Ippy et
Grimary pour la regior. de la Ouaka et a Bangassou pour la region
du Mbomou. Chaque URCES sera composee pendant trois annees d'un
volontaire et d'un expert national; a l'issue de la troisi4me
annge, le volontaire sera remplace par un adjoint;
(c) la formation des cadres des centres pedagogiques r6gionaux (CPR)
et leur participation aux campagnes d'information auprgs des
autorites locales, notamment aupres des communes, directeurs
d'ecoles et APEs pour les sensibiliser aux techniques courantes
d'entretien et les inciter A prendre une part active a la
preservation du patrimoine scolaire; et
(d)  pour concretiser l'engagement solidaire des parents et des
educateurs dans la prise en charge du patrimoine scolaire, il
sera cr66 pour l'Enseignement Fondamental des Conseils
d'Etablissement. En feront partie, a parite de pouvoir d'une
part le bureau de l'APE, d'autre part le Directeur (et les
mattres, le cas echeant). Toutes les decisions y seront prises
conjointement, notamment l'adoption des budgets annuels
d'entretien.  Les fonds contribues seront deposes a un compte
bancaire et tous les raglements effectues par cheque sous la
double signature du President de l'APE et du Directeur de
l1'cole. Les membres de droit du Conseil pourraient y coopter un
ou deux representants de l'autoritg traditionnelle, chef de
village ou notable. Il serait heureux que soient aussi appelgs
au conseil de l1'cole l'un ou l'autre de ces jeunes, rentrgs au
village et qui, jouissant d'un certain prestige, contribuent
souvent aux efforts publics ou prives d'animation des
populations.

2.        Financement.  La reduction de la participation financiere qui
incombe a l'Etat sera accomplie grace:
(a) a la reduction des besoins de remplacement des ecoles et a la
reduction des coOts d'amortissement annuel par gleve, en
favorisant le d6veloppement de techniques de construction a
faibles couits d'investissement initial et d'entretien; ces
dernieres, essentiellement fondees sur l'architecture
traditionnelle, impliqueront un fort coefficient de main-d'oeuvre
locale et dans tout la mesure du possible, l'emploi de materiaux
locaux. Cette strategie a egalement pour effet de limiter lea
apports en devises et l'effet de la hausse des prix des materiaux
importes;
(b)  a l'iimportance donnee aux programmes de construction e:ecutis par
les collectivites;
(c)  au transfert des responsabilit6s en matidre de construction et
d'entretien aux regions et communautes villageoises, ceci
notamment grace a l'appropriation lgale des ecoles par les
villages;
(d)  a l'accroissement des collectes des APEs et a une rationalisation
de l'empiloi de ces fonds. Pour ce faire, il conviendra de
simplifier la collecte de la redevance scolaire, de la concentrer
sur chaque ecole et d'associer les associations de Parents
dVE16ves aux decisions et controles d'utilisation des fonds.
3.        Choix technologigues.  Le deuxieme projet education (Cr. 1359-CA)
a utilise pour la construction/rehabilitation des salles de classe une
technologie bien connue (blocs sable-ciment creux de 15cm sur semelle en
beton arme, dalle en beton, charpente bois, toiture en toles aluminium de
6/10 sur pannes 8x16 avec des pentes de 10% et des menuiserie en panneaux
de bois plein. Pour la mise en oeuvre, les mat6riaux gtaient fournis
directement par le projet, les entreprises locales ne fournissant que la
main-d'oeuvre. L'experience a montre que la centralisation des
approvisionnements (tous les materiaux, a l'exception faite des agregats,
etaient achemin5s A partir de Bangui sur les bases regionales chargees de
les redistribuer ensuite sur les sites), et la c nceltration de la
production des blocs de b6ton dans les bases regionales (chaque base
alimentant les diffgrents chantiers) r6pondaient a un souci de qualitg,
mais en revanche, produisaient des surcouts importants en doublant les
frais de transport et de manutention et g6n6raient m^eme des pertes non
negligeables. En conclusion, il a fte retenu pour le projet propose
(i) d'utiliser davantage les ressources locales; (ii) de reduire autant que
possible les mat6riaux importes; (iii) d'utillser des techniques de
construction simples; (iv) d'obtenir une durabilite des b^atiments d'environ
25 ans; (v) de r6duire les co'uts d'entretien; (vi) d'eviter de centraliser
la fabrication des elements lourds au niveau de Bangui ou dans des bases
regionales; et (vii) lorsque necessaire, de pr6voir l'approvisionnement
directe Pangui-chantiers. Dans cette optique, les technologies suivantes
ont gtg retenues:

(a)  Pour la construction de nouvelles salles de classe: une premiere
intervention est prevue avec le concours d'entreprises
specialisees pour la mise en oeuvre d'une ossature en bois local
(MUKULUNGU dit "bois de fer" pour les pieces principales,
imputrescible et inattaquable par les termines et SAPPELI pour

supportant une toiture en tole galvanisee 6/10 fabriqu6e
localement. Une seconde intervention fera essentiellement appel
