soubassement en pierre, un dallage en terre battue, les murs de
remplissages realises en briques cuites ou en blocs de terre
pressee et les portes en panneaux de bois plein. La salle de
classe mesurant 7mx9m (soit 1.16m2 pour 54 eleves selon les
normes 6ducatives, pouvant descendre jusqu'a 0.79m2 pour 81
6leves en cas d'effectifs pletoriques) sera 6quip6e d'un tableau
noir peint sur enduit ciment, d'un placard incorpore et de 27
tables bancs a deux places (130x60) permettant de recevoir 3
61hves dans des conditions acceptables par le MEN;
(b) Pour la rehabilitation des salles de classe: A l'exception des
paillotes (classees sous la categorie abris provisoires, censees
etre entretenues par la collectivite ou etre remplacges), les
salles de classe (des trois regions du projet) 'en dur" et "en
semi-dur" sont classees en trois categories suivant la gravite
des degats et les modes de construction: (i) petites reparations;
(ii) grosses reparations; et (iii) non reparables pour un cout
raisonnable (si le montant de la reparation devait s'81ever a
plus de la moitig du cout d'une construction neuve de meme
standard). Les petites reparations, notamment pour les classes
de Bangui, comprennent la refection des enduits et peintures et,
surtout la reprise des scellements des menuiseries, des chapes et
le nivellement des abords pour eviter les eaux de ruissellement.
Les grosses reparations, designees par le terme "rehabilitation"
qui representent la majorite des classes rurales comprennent en
plus des interventions precedentes, la remise en etat des
6lements structurels tels que charpentes, toitures, murs fissur6s
et affouillements des fondations. Pour la refection des e6lments
structurels, le mieme type d'ossature bois et couverture que pour
les constructions neuves sera utilis6 afin d'obtenir une
homogeneite du patrimoine a entretenir (les elements modulaires
de charpente ont ete etudies en ce sens pour permettre leur
emploi dans les diverses situations (type de batiments et
dimensions);
(c) Pour l'entretien p6riodique: Apres la rghabilitation des classes
des trois r6gions du projet, la responsabilite de leur entretien
courant et des reparations mineures sera transferee aux
etablissements, en application de la nouvelle politique
prgconisee par le Gouvernement. Dans ce cadre, les conseils
d'etablissemen. recevront les appuis techniques suivants: (i) un
guide d'entretien decrivant les taches regulieres a accomplir et
les manieres de repondre a des degats mineurs; (ii) un livret
d'entretien pour le suivi des performances des responsables

C - Mgthodologie pour la mise en oeuvre du programme de r6habilitation et

1.        Preparation:  Sur financement du Deuxiame Projet et durant la

de mise en oeuvre du projet.
2.        Premiere 6tape: Le projet prevoit la formation initiale
(a) du Directeur de la DCES en matiere de technologie appropri6e et

(b)  du responsable des operations de rehabilitation et d'entretien du

(c) des futurs techniciens des URCES (volontaires et experts

(d) des responsables regionaux (Inspections Acad&miques, CPR,

3.        Deuxieme etape: Mobilisation des ressources communautaires
(a) visite d'information (1 journ6e) Une equipe composee d'un
conseiller de 1'URCES, d'un representant de l'inspection

Sous-Pr6fecture), rencontrera le directeur de l1'cole,
1'Association des Parents d'eleves, le chef du village et les
notables pour leur expliquer les activites envisagees et les
responsabilites des parties concernees. (Une brochure ;ecrivant
le projet leur sera laissee);
(b)  d6liberation de la communaute (4 semaines)  Sous l'autorite du
chef du village, le Directeur de l1'cole et l'Association des

Parents d'elves se rguniront pour etablir la liste des

4.        Troisieme etape: Rehabilitation des ecoles
(a) la DCES assurera la preparation technique des marchgs de genie

Inspections Academiques, les autorites regionales/lozales et la DCES.

PROPOSITION DES STATUS DES CONSEILS D'ECOLE
1.        Objet.  Il est institue dans chacun des etablissements de
l'Enseignement Fondamental, un Conseil d'Ecole charge d'associer le
personnel enseignant et les parents d'eleves, reunis en Association de
Parents d'Eleves (APE), dans la recherche des meilleures conditions du
fonctionnement de l1'cole et de son insertion dans son environnement.
2.        Composition.  Font de droit partie du Conseil d'Ecole, le
Directeur (et le cas echeant les Mattres places sous son autorit6) ainsi
que le bureau de l'APE. Les Niembres de droit peuvent coopteL un ou deux
representants de l'autorite traditionnelle (Chefs de village ou de
quartier) qui ne seralent pas deja Membres de l'APE. Le Responsable de
l'Unite Regionale des Constructions et des Equipements scolaires (URCES)
dont releve l'ecole fait de droit partie du Conseil ou il siege avec voix
consultative. L'Inspecteur d'Academie, l'Inspecteur de l'Enseignement
Fondamental, le Sous-Prefet et le Maire de la Commune dont releve l'ecole
peuvent de droit assister aux reunions du Conseil, et y exprimer les avis
ou faire les communications qu'il estimeraient utiles.
3.        Administration.  Le Conseil est administre conjointement et
solidairement par le bureau de l'APE et le Directeur de l'Ecole. Le
President de l'APE est de droit President du Conseil. En cas d'absence
motivee, il peut se faire suppleer par le Vice-President de l'APE. Le
Directeur de l'Ecole est de droit Vice-Pr6sident du Conseil. Le Tresorier
et le Secrgtaire de l'APE sont de droit respectivement Tr6sorier et
Secretaire du Conseil. Les fonctions de Membre du Conseil sont gratuites.
4.        Ressources.  Les ressources financieres du Conseil sont
constituees par: (i) les cotisations des Membres de l'APE; (ii) les
cotisations des Membres du Conseil non-adherents a l'APE; (iii) les dons et
legs; (iv) les subventions dont il pourrait beneficier de la part de la
Commune ou de toute autre Institution ou Organisme- et (v) les emprunts
qu'il pourrait contracter pour realiser des travaux de reparation ou de
construction dument approuves par l'URCES et l'Inspecteur d'Acadimie.  Les
fonds ainsi reunis sont obligatoirement deposes das leur encaissement par
le Tresorier a un compte bancaire fonctionnant sous la double signature du
President de l'APE et du Directeur de l'Ecole.
5.        Le Conseil a pour missions: (i) d'une part de veiller a la bonne
insertion de l'ecole dans gon environnement; et (ii) d'autre part de
contribuer et faire contribuer les populations des villages et quartiers
servis par l'Ecole A son bon fonctionnement, a la conservation de son
patrimoine, eventuellement a sa renovation ou son extension. En debut
d'annee scolaire, il discute et adopte son budget, qui, s'agissant des
batiments et equipements, lui est propose par l'URCES concernee et doit
prgvoir, en recettes, les cotisations des Membres de l'APE et les autres
recettes 6ventuelles (dons, subventions) et, en depenses, les travaux a
realiser, selon quelles modalites et quel calendrier. En cours d'annee, il
veille a la bonne execution de ce budget: (i) versement aux echeances
prevues des cotisations; (ii) dep^ot des sommes ainsi rentrees au compte
bancaire du Conseil; (iii) reglement des factures et memoires vises par
l'URCES; et (iv) execution des travaux et fournitures en nature.

TERMES DE REFERENCE POUR LE RESPONSABLE DE L'UNITE REGIONALE
DES CONSTRUCTIONS ET DES EQUIPEMENTS SCOLAIRES
DE L'ENSEIGNEMENT FONDAMENTAL
DESCRIPTION DE LA FONCTION
1.        Objet de la fonction.  Veiller A la conservation et a 1'entretien
des batiments et equipements de son secteur d'affectation.
2.        Missions.
(a) Savoir. Le Responsable du secteur connait dans le detail chacune
des ecoles de son secteur qu'il visite p6riodiquement. En
particulier, il s'efforce d'identifier les petits entrepreneurs
susceptibles de contribuer A l'execution des travaux d'entretien
ou d'amelioration des batiments scolaires.
(b) Informer:. A l'occasion de chacune de ses visites, il fait part
au Directeur de l1'cole et au Conseil de l'Ecole de ses
observations concernant la conservation et l'entretien de la
concession, des batiments et de l1equipement scolaires et de ses
recommandations quant aux mesures A prendre pour les assurer. Il
consigne ces observations dans le livret d'entretien de 1'ecole;
il rend compte A l'Inspecteur d'Acad'emie, au Chef du Service
Central d'entretien de la DCES ainsi qu'aux Sous-Prefets et aux
Municipalites concern6s de ces visites et, succinctement, des
observations faites;
(c) Organiser:. Il etablit et met A jour, chaque annee, pour chacune
des ecoles de son secteur, un programme chiffrg decrivart les
travaux A rgaliser immediatement et A prgvoir pour les deux
annees suivantes; il les propose aux Conseils d'Ecole de son
secteur et en discute avec eux les modalites et le calendrier de
financement (cotisation des membres de l'APE, dons, contributions
en nature) et d'execution (en regie ou a l'entreprise) sous sa
mattrise d'oeuvre, les reglements (memoires, factures) etant
assures par le Conseil de l'Ecole, mattre de l'ouvrage sous la
double signature du Directeur de l'Ecole et du President de
l'APE.
(d)  Agir   S'agissant de gros travaux, dgpassant les facultes
contributives locales, il en saisit l'Administration Centrale sur
la base d'un dossier argumentg et chiffre. Il classe ces
operations exceptionnelles par ordre d'urgence et de priorite
d6croissantes: Il en informe l'Inspecteur d'Acadgmie et le
Sous-Prefet concerne; et il etablit et transmet par la voie
hirarchique la demande de dotation budgetaire de son secteur au
titre du chapitre du Budget de l'Etat "entretien des ecoles
primaires". Il met en oeuvre de faCon appropriee, les moyens en
personnel et en materiel dont il dispose directement, soit pour
des interventions ponctuelles, soit en concourant A l'ex6cution
des travaux pris en charge par les Conseils d'Ecole; et il
contacte directement, ou par tout autre canal approprie les



- 106 -
ANNEX 3-4
Page 11 of 19
entrepreneurs locaux susceptibles de concourir a l'execution des
travaux, negocie les conditions de leur intervention, les guide
et les controle pendant celle-ci.
(e)  Gerer.  Il g8re les personnel, mat6riels et credits dont dispose
irentitg administrative dont il est responsable et il surveille
le bon emploi des moyens financiers et mat6riels reunis pour
l'execution des travaux dont il assure la mattrise d'oeuvre, quel
qu'en soit le mode de financement et d'exgcution.
3.        Liaisons
(a)  Le Responsable de l'URCES releve higrarchiquement de l'Inspecteur
d'Academie et techniquement de la DCES; et
(b)  Il entretient des relations fonctionnelles avec les Inspecteurs
de l'Enseignement Fondamental, les CPR, lea Directeurs d'ecole,
les Associations de Parents d'Eltve et les Municipaliths de son
secteur.
4.        Profil
(a)  Titulaire du BTS Batiment ou d'une qualification equivalente,
voire sup6rieure;
(b)  Au moins trois annees de pratique professionnelle du batiment, de
preference dans les travaux d'entretien;
(c)  Aptitude a agir de faCon autonome; et
(d)  Grandes qualites de relations humaines et de persuasion.
5.        Conditions d'emploi.  Contractuel pour une duree de trois annees
(renouvelable), ii disposera d'un vehicule et construira son propre
logement la premiare annge sur financement du projet. Il travaillera en
6quipe avec un homologue.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUPPORT TO THE INSPECTORATE SYSTEM
1.        Background.  The Ministry of National Education has decentralized
its operational resr nsibilities for the control and management of secondary
and primary educatio. (including the CPRs), from the Secondary Education
Directorate (Direction des Enseignements Secondaire et Techniques - DEST) and
the Primary Education Directorate (Direction Enseignement Prescolaire et
Fondamental 1 - DEPF 1) to, at the regional level, five academic inspector-
ates (IAs), and at the local level -- for primary education -- to 19 Primary
Inspectorates (IEFs) (see Chart 1). The three original academic inspector-
ates, created at the end of the school year 1985-1986, have proved their
worth w.th their capacity to: (a) undertake regular field visits to the
educational establishments in their areas; (b) report quickly on urgent
matters to the Ministry headquarters; (d) liaise with local government repre-
sentatives; and (e) resolve managerial problems without referral to the
capital.  Difficulties remain.  The academic inspections are not yet properly
installed in their regions (their accomodation remains a problem) and the
primary inspections do not have the transport or operating budget to carry
out regular inspection, supervision or control of the system.
2.        The educational reform, discussed in detail in 1984, revised the
profiles for primary school inspectors and pedagogic councillors. The decree
of January 1986, modified the organizational structure of the MEN, defined
the operational duties of academic inspections, but left the possibility for
further precision in their role following their initial operation. The
organizational structure, the vertical and horizontal relationships between
MEN, the academic inspections, and the primary inspections have been fully
defined, but the demarkation of administrative responsibilities for areas
such as personnel management are not yet finalized. The definition of
detailed responsibilities will result from the work undertaken under this and
other components of the project.
3.        The work of the primary inspectors is well defined, with a calendar
of reports to be submitted to MEN during the school year, and an indication
of the administrative tasks and documents for which the inspector has respon-
sibility. The major items include: statements of recruitment, absence, and
leave for education personrnel; the report on the start of the school year;
listing of educational personnel by district; listing of school directors;
listing of administrative personnel; reports on schoil inspections and class
visits; school statistics; report on staffing movements, including proposals
for promotion and candidates for professional examinations; reports on the
pupil examination centers, and the conduct of the pupil examinations; and the
end of the school year report.
4.        Regarding pedagogical aspects, the support and development of
teachers is the responsibility of both the primary school inspectorate and
the CRPs. These functions are largely ignored by the inspectorate because



- 116 -
ANNEX 3-5
Page 2 of 9
the means of making school supervision visits are limited, with a reported
total of only 8 of the 19 inspectorate vehicles operating, and all of these
vehicles being in poor states of repair (they date from either 1978 or the
UNICEF program of 1980). A lack of document reproduction and other facili-
ties hampers the operation of the inspectorate staff. Furthermore, the
absence of in-service training for the inspectors and pedagogical advisors
has made it difficult for them to keep abreast of the new pedagogical devel-
opments and introduce and support innovative teaching methods.
5.        Obiectives.  This component would increase the effectiveness of the
decentralized administration of the education system, especially with respect
to the primary level through support to the academic inspectorates and the
local primary school inspectorates. Sub-objectives include:
(a) at MEN level, making better and more frequent use of the IA
reports;
(b) making the IAs operational by speeding up their effective material
installation;
(c)  redistributing regionally the daily management tasks (control of
enrollments, personnel allocations and placement/postings, follow-
up of equipment and maintenance of schools, administrative and
pedagogical management of schools, local consultation, etc.);
(d) re-activating the local level primary inspectorates, and providing
them with the means to fulfill their responsibilities; and
(e) reorganizing the school timetable to make better use of resources
and to allow a gradual reduction in the average class-size of
primary schools from 62 to 55 by 1993/94.
6.        Approach/strategy.  The Government has attached major importance to
the role to be played by the academic and primary inspectorates i decentra-
lizing educational management and reactivating the control and supervisory
functions, and during 1987, will increase the number of academic inspector-
ates from 3 to 5. The roles and responsibilities of the academic inspections
are being clarified and refined as a body of experience builds up with regard
to the operation of the firt,t IAs. The profile and tasks defined for the
primary inspector are still valid, it remains only to provide the decentral-
ized organs of the MEN the operational means to carry out their responsibi-
lities. The results of the personal management reorganization, the major
statistical survey of the education sector, the reinforcement of the academic
inspections, and the decentralization of the DCES, will allou the Government
to improve its support and control of rural primary schools through greater
decentralization of the management of the educational system to the regional
and local levels. The means and retraining needed will be provided under the
project.
7.        ProblemAi remain, especially in the transition period 1987-1988.
During this time, a working group will continue to refine the distribution of
managerial responsibilities, and inspectors and pedagogical advisors will
follow an in-service retraining program. As regards the use of transport, to
promote the regular inspection of schools, and to reactivate the reporting



- 117 -
ANNEX 3-5
Page 3 of 9
function of primary inspectors, small capacity motorcycles and mopeds will be
provided for each primary inspection. In addition, a limited number of
vehicles, with an operating budget, will be held at each academic inspection
for the use, upon request, of the primary inspectors, for specific tasks.
These vehicles will be for the exclusive use of the primary inspectorates but
the academic inspector will release the vehicle only for an agreed purpose
and the primary inspector will be obliged to provide a written report, for
submission to the academic inspector, on the substantive task undertaken
during the mission. Should the report not be forthcoming, or prove unsatis-
factory, the primary inspector will be denied access to the vehicle pool
until the situation is redressed.
8.        In conjunction with the inservice teachers training program and the
special program for primary school directors, this components would address
the problem of the large average class-size of primary school through a
revised and optimised school timetable. By the introduction of multi-grade
teaching, double shift schooling and a more efficient programming of the
s^~hool day, combined with a minor increase in the number of hours effectively
taught by each teacher, the average class-size will be reduced from 62 to 55
by 1993194. The scope for additional reductions during this period is
limited because of the number of teachers absent from the classroom following
the inservice training program. However, upon completion of the inservice
program and redeployment of the non-teaching staff back into classrooms,
further reduction of average class-size to a figure of about 50 are
anticipated.
9.        Timetable.  The distribution of the component activities over time
is given in the attached detailed tables.
10.       Administration and Manaxement.  The responsibility for the imple-
mentation of this component is entrusted initially to the Project Coordina-
tion Unit, and thence directly to the academic inspections. The interests of
the primary school inspections will be represented by the participation of
the Primary Education Directorate ("Direction Enseignement Prescolaire et
Fondamental 1").
11.       Means and Costs.  To assist in making the above changes opera-
tional, the project would finance: (a) building rehabilitation for 3 IAs
located at Bangui Ville, Bambari, and Bangassou; (b) a supply of office
furniture and equipment for the same 3 IAs; (c) a total of 18 vehicles for
the use of the IEFs, together with the required maintenance and fuel costs;
(d) office operating expenditures for 3 lAs and 19 IEFs; (e) 38 light
motorcycles and mopeds, together with their related operating costs for the
IEFs; and (f) in-service training and seminars for 70 inspectors and
pedagogical advisors. The following table provides brief details of the
short in-service training program for the primary inspectors and pedagogical
advisors. Details of the quantities, programming and costs of this component
are also given in the attached tables.



- 118 -
ANNEX 3-5
Page 4 of 9
Table 1 - In-Service Training for the Primary School Inspectorates
Title of Course             Specialist workshops on educational administration and management
Objectives of Course
1. Main                  Improve decentralized educational management and inspection
2. Sub-objectives        Reinforce the regional and local level inspectorates and promote
better coordination between MEN and academic inspectorates, and
academic Inspectorates and primary school inspectorates.
3. Required outcomes    Greater decentralization, improved administration and regional
and district management of education
Preparation, Staffing       Preparation:  six local consultants during one week of 1988.  The
and Management             consultants would include:  staff of the Inspectorate General,
staff of ENAM, MEN and CPRAC staff. Teaching: as above with
academic and MEN participation (total of 10 persons).
Admissions criteria         Restricted to staff of the academic inspectorates, the primary
school inspectorates, and not more~ than 15 MEN headquarters staff.
Enrollment                  70 participants
Duration                    Five days only (one week)
Frequency                   Once every two years in 1988, 1990, 1992
Timing                      One month prior to the start of the school year
Location                    CNFC Bangui
Instructional Program
1. Main topic            Decentralized educational administration
- purpose, elements, roles, responsibilities
2. Subsidiary topics    Personnel management
- attendance, leave, transfers, promotion, notation,
performance, reporting, sanctions and discipline
Statistics and planning
- statistics collection, definitions, verification, collation
and calculation, pupil growth, opening of new schools and or
classes, school location and local community involvement
Reporting and inspection
- record keeping, report writing, annual program of essential
reports and correspondence
Finance
- budgeting and financial mobilization, cost control and
financial management, basic accounting
Examinations
- organization and administration, confidentiality, security

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
IMPROVEMENTS IN HIGHER EDUCATION QUALITY, PLANNING AND FINANCE
1.        Background.  The evolution of the student population at the
University of Bangui from 1984-1986 shows that enrollments have grown at
almost 7% p.a. over the three years to 2,628 in 1985/86. The number of
students and staff by institute and faculty is shown in Table !. The
dominance of arts based subjects, which have almost 82% of the students, is
evident.
Table I - University of Bangui
Student Numbers         Staff (permanent)
1984/85      1985/86      1984/85      1985/86
A.        FACULTIES                    1/            2/
-Humanities / Social Science          657           686          39           38
-Law and Economics                    800         1,071           19          17
-Health Sciences                      426           309           13           7
-Pure Sciences                         97            86           22          21
B.    "GRANDES ECOLES"
-Polytechnic                           53            74            3           3
-Business Management Unit             135           100            5           5
-Rural Development Inst.               74            79           12          13
-Teacher Education Inst.              280           223           28          14
Total                               2,522         2,628          141         118
of which non-nationals                262           295          66           52
1/ end of the academic year
2/ beginning of the academic year
Source: Mission estimates and MEN Statistical Yearbook.
The breakdown of the higher education budget for the period 1985-1987
(Table 2) shows that poor resource allocation hampers the efficient operation
and development of the university. Less than 3% is allocated for materials,
and at least one third of this is spent, not on pedagogical supplies or
educational materials, but rather on the operation of the student restaurant
and the hiring of temporary teaching staff. The CAR has very few bilateral
funded scholarships (France transformed its scholarship program into
budgetary support and an in-service training/employment program in 1986) and
as a consequence, two thirds of the higher education budget is used to fund
scholarships. Of the total scholarship budget (estimated at 1,373 million
CFAF for the 12 months of 1987), 57% was used to fund scholarships abroad for
only 30% of the award holders. As a consequence, the University of Bangui



- 125 -
ANNEX 3-6
Page 2 of 11
has been starved of operating resources. Specifically, the university
library, which was started only in 1980 and has one place for every 25
students, is both overcrowded and has a limited stock of works, many of which
out of date or not relevant to the courses taught at the university. A
similar situation exists in the science faculty, where the original equipment
(from a bilateral donor in 1970) is obsolete, broken and spare parts are
unavailable.
Table 2 - Higher Education Expenditure
(millions CFAF)
1985             1986           1987
Total expenditure                  1,883            2,133           2,073
Percentage distrib.
Materials                        2%               2%              3%
Salaries                       25%              25%             25%
Scholarships                   68%              67%             67%
Other                            5%               6%             6%
Total                          100%             100%           100%
Source: Mission estimates.
2.        Data on the labor force and the employment situation are scarce,
out of date and particularly unsystematic and unreliable e.g., a survey of
industry in the early 1980s was answered by only 20 firms of which 5 provided
information which was not contradictory. The employment statistics for 1981
show: 85% of the labor force engaged in the primary economic sector; 1% in
the secondary sector; and 4% in the tertiary sector, with the public service
employing over 26,000 people. Recruitment into the civil service has been
substantially reduced with the application of the finance law (3 francs of
savings for every 1 franc of new recruitment), but the mismatch between the
output of the education system and the employment market is not only quanti-
tative. The distortion in higher education which favors arts based subjects
(over 70% of scholarship awards are in these fields) has led to over-
production of graduates in humanities. The high level manpower requirements
of the CAR economy have not been quantified, nevertheless, it is apparent
that the demand for university trained manpower is limited, narrow and un-
likely to expand rapidly in the immediate future. This poses several
problems for the higher education system. First, the overwhelming need to
improve the quality of higher education in the CAR, both in terms of student
achievement and In terms of their relevance to the needs of the economy; this
implies qualitative improvements to the university and the means to adapt the
curreiit courses to short-term training. Second, the importance of avoiding
high cost overseas training which has drained higher education resources
thereby starving the university in the CAR. Third, the need to reduce the
overall output of higher education graduates to avoid serious graduate
unemployment.

3.        Objectives and Means.  In order to assist the university adapt to
the labor market conditions, while reducing the social expenditures on higher
education, the project will provide inputs for the qualitative improvement of
higher education while assisting the Government put into place a scholarship
system designed to respond to the country's needs and take account of its
resources. Specifically, the project will:
(a) improve study facilities artd limited library space with: (i) the
doubling of the number of places in the university library with an
extention of the existing building; and (ii) an increase of 50% in
the number of works available from the library;
(b) renovate limited facilities for experimental work in science based
subjects with: (i) minor repairs to the science faculcy building;
and (li) the provision of a stock of basic laboratory equipment and
consumable supplies for the areas of mathematics, physics,
chemistry, biology and geology;
(c) support the university planning office with: (i) a micro computer
and office equipment; and (ii) specialist services to assist in
programming of short courses and the preparation of the university
development plan (computer programming, planning and budgeting,
student statistics and quota allocations);
(d) reinforce and develop the National Scholarship Service (SAD)
charged with administering the scholarship award system with:
(i) renovation of the office buildings, basic office equipment,
transport and furniture; (ii) micro computing facilities to
computerize the scholarship system; (iii) specialist services and
consultants to undertake specific studies on the success rates of
students in France, the elaboration of the quota system, the



- 127 -
ANNEX 3-6
Page 4 of 11
computerization of the scholarship system, the labor market and its
high level manpower requirements, and planning and budgeting; and
(iv) training and study visits for the staff of the SAB.
4.        Approach/strategy.  One of the key targets of the Action Plan (see
Annex 3-1) is to reduce the proportion of the education sector budget spent
on scholarships from its current figure of 17% to 9% by the end of the
project. This can be achieved in two stages; first, a transition phase which
includes: the application of the existing texts and regulations governing the
award of new and the renewal of ongoing scholarships; the continuation of a
careful purge of the scholarship lists to exclude all fraud, double counting,
repetitions, and other anomalies; and the completion of the preliminary
studies required to develop a coherent and comprehensive quota allocation
system (the analysis of student success rates at the University of Bangui and
those with scholarships in France; detailed studies on the additional costs
of transport, lodging, social security, reciprocal inscription costs for
students abroad; and the elaboration of the draft texts needed for the
revised scholarship award system). Second, the implementation of the defini-
tive quota system which will govern the award of scholarships, together with
the computerization of scholarship records.
5.        The budgetary targets included in the Actirn Plan for the scholar-
ship program indicate that there will be a 35% nominal reduction, or a 50%
real reduction in the allocation for scholarships during the life of the
project. This reduction will be applied to the overseas scholarship alloca-
tion prior to any reduction in the amounts allocated for study at the
University of Bangui. Table 4 simulates the effect of: reducing the overall
scholarship allocation of the education sector budget from 17% to 9%;
reducing the proportion of the scholarship budget spent in France from 42% to
20%; holding the proportion of the budget spent abroad elsewhere at 15%; and
increasing the amount allocated to the Urniversity of Bangui from 43% to 65%.
The total number of scholarships declines from 2,200 to 1,780, and the number
abroad from 650 to 270, while the scholarships at the University of Bangui
remain stable at 1,500.

6.         The effort required from higher education to adapt to the new labor
market conditions and to reduce social expenditures will be that much easier
to make if resources are made available to improve the quality of higher edu-
cation. Hence, the improvement of the library and the science faculty of the
University of Bangui will be started prior to the implementation of the new
scholarship quota system.
7.         Timetable.  The details of the implementation calendar for this
component are given in the attached tables.
8.         Administration and Management.  For all activities relating to the
University of Bangui, the Ministry of Higher Education is the responsible
department. Daily implementation will be managed by the Rectorate of the
University, supported as necessary by the Project Coordination Unit located
In the MEN. As regards to the scholarship system, and the support and devel-
opment of the SAB, although this service is attached to the Presidency,
management responsibility will rest with SAB, assisted in procurement and
project accounting by the Project Coordination Unit of MEN, and by the DCES
for minor building repairs.
9.         Means and Costs.  Full details and costs of all inputs are provided
in the attached tables.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
PRIMARY EDUCATION DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUPPORT TO HUMAN RESOURCE MANAGEMENT
1.        Background.  With a staff of about 6,000 (1986) and a budget
equivalent to about one-fourth of the national total, the Ministry of
National Education (MEN) is the largest government department. However, as
with the rest of the civil service, in the early 1980s, MEN was, and still
largely is, plagued with numerous problems: (a) overstaffing and skills
mismatch, due to supply driven recruitment; (b) inefficiency due to lack of
clearly defined objectives, functions and criteria, poor coordination, and
excessive regulations; (c) fraud and low productivity due to a lack of
controls and operating means; and (d) demotivation due to the lack of profes-
sional opportunities and (since 1982) frozen salaries. All these factors
have contributed to the low quality of education.
2.        To address these problems for the whole civil service, in 1982 the
Government launched a Public Administration Reform (PAR) aimed to: (a) re-
duce the administration's operating expenses, especially the salary bill,
which had reached an unsustainable level; (b) improve the administration/
financial management of government personnel; (c) streamline government
structures; and (d) upgrade the skills of civil servants. This PAR, which is
being implemented under supervision of a "Permanent Reform Council" (Conseil
Permanent de la Reforme Administrative Centrafrication - CPRAC), has begun to
bear fruit, with a reduction of the Government salaries/budgetary receipts
ratio from 89.3% in 1981 to 58% in 1985 (the objective is 50% by 1990).
Given the size of MEN, improving the management of its human resources and
restoring a career structure as part of PAR would markedly promote global
efficiency and cost saving objectives; it would enhance the ability of the
education system to produce graduates who are better equipped for direct
employment or for further training. Finally, it would provide a model for
other departments.
3.        Objectives.  In line with IDA's recently approved structural
adjustment credit, this component would support the implementation of PAR by
improving personnel management in the Ministry of Education with two
objectives: (a) containing the growth of the wage element of educational
unit costs and reducing administrative staff costs in favor of teacher costs;
and (b) improving the efficiency of MEN's staff. In turn, these would be
important ingredients for a successful implementation of the country's 1984
education reform. Intermediate objec.ives under (a) are to: (i) eliminate
fraud; (ii) enforce the existing, recently simplified, regulations concerning
retirement, family allowances, cost of living adjustments, etc.;
(iii) exercise budgetary controls; and (iv) increase the teacher/non-teacher
ratio. Under (b), they are to: (i) streamline organization and procedures;
(ii) increase productivity through improved supervision; (iii) increase
professionalism through training; (iv) ensure greater continuity by reducing
staff rotation; and (v) increase motivation and competition through
non-salary incentives.



- 136 -                      ANNEX 3-7
Page 2 of 9
4.        Expected outcome.  Through a strict application of existing regu-
lation (retirement age, sanctions, rule making new recruitment conditional
upon previous savings on personnel in the ratio of 1:3), this project com-
ponent is expected to result in a somewhat leaner institution (with staff
numbers reduced by 3-5%); more importantly, the skill profile of staff would
be enhanced, as new recruitment would focus on individuals with skills not
yet found, and badly needed, such as planners, policy analysts, economists
and statisticians, to sharper the Ministry's strategy formulation capacity.
The wage element of costs is expected to marginally increase, from 80% (1987)
to 82% (1993) of MEN's total recurrent expenditures because newly recruited
staff will be more qualified. Containing the growth of the total salary bill
will allow budgetary increases to be allocated to quality inputs such as
pedagogic materials or teacher in-service training. This, combined with
improved staff morale and competition due to increased career opportunities
and more rigorous supervision, will provide the conditions for improvements
in the quality of education, Government's main objective for the sector.
5.        Approach/strategy.  As a first step towards rationalization, in
1986 MEN adopted a new, streamlined organization chart which underlines the
central role of the Minister's cabinet for strategic thinking and policy
formulation and clearly separates administrative from pedagogic functions.
In addition, the strategy to improve the Ministry's management combines
government-wide measures with MEN-specific ones, aimed at increasing controls
on one hand, and incentives on the other. These include: (a) the computeri-
zation of MEN's personnel functions; (b) the preparation and implementation
of a staffing plan and the linking of MEN's budget to authorized posts; and
(c) the introduction of measures to further rationalize MEN's structures,
improve staffing and increase efficiency; the most critical of these measures
would form part of the Action Plan.
6.        During project preparation, the computerization of MEN's Personnel
and Budget Directorates started, as part of PAR, with the assistance of the
National Computer Center ("Office National d'Informatique" - ONI) and of
internationally recruited consultants. This activity includes: (a) a
complete check of all personnel records and their transfer first to a new,
more precise manual filing system, then to a computer data system (January -
October 1988); (b) the purchase of two micro-computers (January 1988);
(c) the merging and transfer to MEN, for each civil servant, of the computer
databases for performance records (as updated by MEN), salary history (previ-
ously held in the Ministry of Finance), and career information (previously
held in the Ministry of Civil Service) (January - March 1988); (d) creation
of a data processing unit under IDA's Second Technical Assistance Project
(IDA Cr. 1581CA), with UNDP funding of a full-time computer specialist and a
programmer, to meet the data processing needs of the Ministry at large (July
- August 1987); and (e) initial training of the Personnel Directorate staff
(September - December 1987) under joint USAID-Canada financing.
7.        The staffing plan was conducted by a team consisting of MEN's
Personnel staff, with the assistance of a Canadian consulting firm who has
been associated with this exercise for several other ministries in CAR. They
analyzed MEN's objectives, its organlzation and procedures and the profile of
each civil servant in relation with the tasks he/she is expected to perform.
The problem areas identified included:



- 137 -                      ANNEX 3-7
Page 3 of 9
(a)  structures:  although the 1986 organigram represents a substantial
improvement over the previous one, a number of units (notably, the
Directorates of Planning, Pedagogic Research, and External Rela-
tions) are still not fully integrated into MEN's mainstream, or
suffer from an unclear mandate and ill-defined procedures;
(b) staffing: 100 out of 325 posts in MEN were identified as redundant
(Table 1), and the proportion of non-teachers in the sector was
found to be excessive (11%), especially in secondary education,
where it reaches 27%; the utilization of human resources is sub-
optimal, as shown by the extensive use of expensive long-term
contract employees or overtime by existing civil servants, in areas
for which young graduates have adequate qualifications, and by the
large number of teachers (about 200) waiting to be assigned for
duty, or retirees still on the active duty payroll. Qualitatively,
the Ministry is overwhelmingly staffed with teachers who have not
been trained to perform administrative tasks; and
(c) operating efficiency: productivity is low at various levels for
different reasons. Among the administrative staff, this is due to
a lack of information about MEN's objectivea, insufficient delega-
tion of authority and accountability, poor or no administrative
training (sub-para. (b) above), compounded by a lack of financial
rewards for exercising responsibility and takirng risks (the average
net salary range in the public sector is only from 20,000 CFA for a
primary education graduate to 110,000 CFA for a higher education
graduate). Poor internal and external information and coordina-
tion, and the lack of data are additional constraints. Among
teachers, poor performance results from insufficient support and
control by inspectors, low motivation due to the elimination of
professional exams and in-service training opportunities and a
rating system which leaves considerable room for favoritism.
8.        On the basis of this diagnosis, organization, staffing and perform-
ance norms were set by MEN. In order to implement them, an advisor from
CPRAC was seconded to MEN as Personnel Director, and steps are proposed in
each of these three areas. First, the Ministry is in the process of further
streamlining its organization chart. The pedagogic research and teacher
training Directorate will be reorganized to avoid duplication between INRAP
and CNFC and its permanent staff will be pared down in favor of short-term
specialists to allow more flexibility in meeting evolving training
priorities. The role and authority of the Administration and Personnel
Directorate (DAP) will be strengthened principally through a revision of
promotion procedures and an improvement of its coordination with the rest of
the Ministry.
9.        Second, the Action Plan will include targets and indicators con-
cerning staff numbers and profile (Details on the staff reduction and reas-
signment program are given in Table 2 while Table 3 provides an estimate of
potential savings and their proposed use). A small Training Unit (one higher
level individual, one support staff) would be created within DAP, with well
defined procedures. The training officer, as well as other DAP personnel
responsible for this component, would receive specialized training and



- 138 -                      ANNEX 3-7
Page 4 of 9
technical assistance in computer operation, personnel management and the
design of training programs. Each year the Training Unit would, as part of
the Action Plan, and on the basis of identified needs, design and arrange a
program aimed at upgrading the skills of managers, technical staff, and
support personnel. Managers would, together with their colleagues from other
ministries, attend seminars regularly organized under PAR by the Central
African "Ecole Nationale d'Administration et de Management" (ENAM) with
assistance from Quebec's Ecole Nationale d'Administration Publique (ENAP).
Inspectorate staff, pedagogic advisers, school directors and teachers would
receive in-service training organized at CNFC and CPRs by the Directorate of
teacher training under project financing. Organization and motivation
seminars would be offered in-house for administrative and clerical personnel,
using specialized local consultants. Because of its low cost, the latter
item would not be included in the project.
10.       Third, to improve administrative efficiency, at the first annual
review, Government would submit detailed operating procedures, revised to:
(a) decentralize to the academic inspectorates an increased degree of
decisiotu power regarding routine staffing matters; (b) reduce staff turnover
to 10% annually; (c) introduce regular staff meetings as well as management
and staff information procedures; and (d) establish a properly timed, well
designed, annual data collection operation to meet the information needs of
the various directorates. To improve teacher efficiency, the inspection
process would be strengthened, an in-service training system would be set up,
and incentives such as the reinstatement of a professional competitive exam
for teachers are being envisaged.
11.       Timetable.  Table 2 provides the timeframe and other details on
this component.  The Action Plan, agreed upon at negotiations, will be
subject to yearly joint Government-IDA review. After the end of the project,
it is expected that the Action Plan and the monitoring of a number of input
and output indicators will have become routine management techniques of MEN.
12.       Administration and management.  Responsibility for this component
will be entrusted to DAP, properly backed up by ONI and by functional short-
term technical assistance. A modest training program design capacity will be
created within DAP. The DAP team, as well as the Budget Directorate, will
report to the Minister through the Director General of Administration and
Finance. It will liaise closely with MEN's Data Process'ig Unit, the Reform
Council and the Ministry of Civil Service, to ensure thaL PAR implementation
in the Ministry of Education proceeds in line with Government-wide plans.
13.       Means.  The project will finance for DAP:  (a) furniture, equipment
and office supplies; (b) 24 manmonths of scholarships abroad in the areas of
computer science, personnel management, and training piogram design; (c) 6
manmonths of local training to initiate staff in computerized personnel
management techniques; (d) 6 man-months of short-term consult:nt services to
provide initial assistance in (i) operating the new personnel management
system; and (ii) designing and organizing the staff training program.
14.       Detailed costs.  Cost estimates for this component are based on
specific unit costs which have been standardized throughout the project
(DATGEN). Table 4 provides details.

Table 3 - Potential Savings on the Szlary Bill as per MEN's Staffing Plan and Their Proposed Use
The figures shown have been estimated using a combination of physical controls, computer cross-checks and institutional analysis. In
some cases they are only rough indications, given to provide a sense of what would be theoretically feasible.

(CFAF m)

"Vacataires" 2/                                     440         0.25               110         Reduction by 113                       36
"Contractuels" 3/                                   107         1.16               124        Reduction by 1/3                        41

Total                       1,152*                                                                             488
* There may be some overlap between the various staff categories.
1/   As civil servants retire, their pensions are paid by the Treasury out of the Government's Pension Fund, and their salaries repre-
sent a saving for MEN's budget.  For the years ahead, the computer listing projects the following number of retirees:  1988 - 48;
1989 = 60; 1990 = 56; 1991 - 85; 1992 = 87.
2/   "Vacataires" are civil servants wbo cumulate a full-time job and additional services on a part-time basis, i.e., almost
exclusively MEN employees teaching at the University of Bangui. In a number of cases, comparable qualifications are found among
young graduates who are unemployed. Government is reviewing the possibility of substituting those young graduates for some of the
"vacataires".
3/  "Contractuels" are fixed-term  employees, whose contracts have been renewed many times, although their posts are sometimes
redundant. Under the staffing plan, the positions which cannot be justified will be closed, and others will be filled by
recruiting young "stagiaires contractuels" (i.e., graduates awaiting recruitment in the civil service).
4/   Staff identified as "redundant" (about 120 in the Ministry itself- see Table 1) will be made available to an existing Go-trnment-
wide "Personnel Bank" and will: (1) either be retrained under the PAR (cf para. 9 in Annex 3-3); or (ii) encouraged to leave with
a severance payment (however, this voluntary departure program has remained a deadletter due to a lack of funding); or (tit) placed
in another Government department or development organization, depending on their skills.
5/ In addition to the staffing plan, MEN has started to: (1) identify, through the inspectorates, and through physical and medical
controls in the field, all its employees guilty of serious professional misconduct (absenteeism, alcoholism) with a view to
dismissing them; and (ii) monitor closely staff members receiving long-tern practical training abroad, to eliminate gross
excesses. Ranges of 100-150 individuals for category (i), and 50-90 for category (11) have been quoted, but theF are not yet
based on hard evidence and should be treated with caution. Therefore the lower end of the range has been used.
6/  As a priority, Government proposes to use whatever savings it can achieve to recruit "stagiaires contractuels", i.e., young gradu-
ates awaiting recruitment in the civil service (about 750). With savings of about CFAF 488 million, application of the 1:3 rule
would theoretically allow new hiring equivalent to about CFAF 163 million p.a.; this amount could finance the recruitment of about
271 (or 36% of the "stagiaires contractuels", whose salaries average CFAF 0.6 million p.a.).  Because these young people are
generally more qualified and eager to work, such a move would enhance the profile and productivity of MEN.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
FINANCIAL AND PHYSICAL RESOURCE MANAGEMENT
1.        Background.  The Ministry of National Education has almost one-
third of the total civil servants in the Central African Republic, and the
education sector consumes over one-quarter of the Government's recurrent
expenditure budget. In spite of this impressive responsibility, the devel-
opment of the MEN's managerial, administrative and analytic capacities have
kept pace neither with the growth in size and complexity of the sector, nor
with recent developments in managerial techniques and tools. To rdapt the
administrative structure, the Government revised the internal organization of
the MEN in January 1986, and during 1986-87, a subsequent revision of the
budgetary nomenclature was made to reflect these internal changes. These
improvements, while necessary, have not been sufficient to ensure the desired
improve-.ent in the management of financial and physical resources.
2.        Problems which exist at present in the financial management of the
sector include: (a) a lack of coherent, systematically applied financial
procedures; (b) poor budgeting and accounting procedures; (c) weak database
and information systems; (d) lack of trained and experienced staff; and
(e) inadequate office space and equipment. Budget preparation, in the last
few years, has been principally determined by the need to limit expenditures
and has left very little initiative to MEN. Personnel expenses are dictated
by recruitments and promotions, both frozen since 1982. Domestic investment
funds have remained little more than symbolic and consequently only material
expenditures retained some quasi-flexibility. However, even in this area,
the very tight expenditure controls have not allowed more than small changes
in one line item, on condition that an equal but opposite change was made in
an item elsewhere. The reality is that it has been almost impossible to
reduce existing budgetary lines, especially to fund a new budget item, with
the result that most new budgetary items receive a token allocation, far
below their needs, and this allocation remains small, especially in com-
parison with historic budget lines.
3.        With respect to the managenient of physical resources, the problems
include: (a) sparse, fragmented and unreliable data on the condition of the
school buildings, furniture and materials; (b) conflict in the responsi-
bilities for the control and management of educational buildings;
(c) inefficient purchasing and procurement procedures; (d) limited storage
and distribution facilities; and (e) rapid deterioration of physical
facilities owing to lack of maintenance and capacity to plan its financing.
4.        Objectives.  The main objective of this component is to improve
resource allocation and management, especially in the areas of: (a) budget
preparation, financial management, accounting, cost monitoring and expendi-
ture control; (b) general procurement procedures, bulk purchasing and
contract management; (c) management and control of the physical resources of
the education sector; and (d) the strengthening of the database systems



- 145 -
ANNEX 3-8
Page 2 of 6
needed for resource management. The objective of the proposed budgetary
breakdown is to allow MEN to compensate for the past lack of any coherent
budget allocation criteria, especially for materials, since this category
will triple during the project period.
5.        This component would support the creation of a management infor-
mation system for the control and monitoring of financial and physical
resources. The required outcomes of this component include: (a) wide
coverage of financial areas in the management database system; (b) improved
allocation and more efficient use of limited resources; (c) an improved
financial management capacity, with more realistic budgeting and accurate
accounting; and (d) reduction in the costs of materials through improved
procurement procedures.
6.        Approach/Strategy.  The project would assist in strengthening the
key directorates and units of the MEN involved in resource allocation and
management. These include: (a) the Cabinet, which controls the data proces-
sing unit; (b) the General Secretariat, which coordinates administrative
matters in the MEN; and (c) the General Directorate of Administration and
inance, which controls the Personnel Directorate, the Budget and Accounting
Directorate, and the School Construction and Equipment Directorate. A
working party of the senior managers of the MEN concerned with resource
allocation, as listed above, will assist in defining the key criteria to be
adopted during the annual budget preparation.
7.        With respect to budgetary allocations for materials, instead of
proceeding with pro-rata increases for each budgetary line item, more
objective criteria must be introduced to take into consideration the size and
nature of the institution or unit under consideration. Three simple criteria
are proposed. Firstly, for schools and institutions, the criteria should be
based on the number of pupils or students (a small lycee should have less
than a large lycee; and, more importantly, there should be a certain pro-
portionality between the credits and the enrollment). Secondly, for central
administration services, the indicator should be the number of staff (a unit
of 100 persons will require more than a unit of 10). Thirdly, for transpor-
tation operating expenditures (fuel and maintenance costs for vehicles) the
priority will be given to those services whose task requires mobility
(inspectorates) as opposed to the central services.
8.        The project would create a physical facility management unit within
the DGAF to maintain and manage existing infrastructure and its financing
through increased local authority and community participation; jointly with
DCES, it would maintain a computerized Inventory of existing facilities in
the three project regions, decide on basic construction/equipment/maintenance
standards, approve curricula on basic maintenance techniques for teacher
training and prepare the rolling triennial plan for school construction,
rehabilitation and maintenance. The technical advisor of the URCES, posted
in each of the three regions, would serve as liaison between the APEs,
communities, academic inspectorates, DCES and DGAF. With respect to
procurement procedures, the project would help DGAF strengthen its capacity
through a task force which would be responsible for the preparation of
standard documents to bring the procurement procedures in CAR in line with
the Bank procedures.



- 146 -
ANNEX 3-8
Page 3 of 6
9.        Timetable and costs.  The implementation calendar is given in the
attached summary tables which also detail the quantities and base costs of
the elements financed by the project.
10.       Administration and Management.  Responsibility for the implemen-
tation of the different elements of the component would be given to the
Cabinet for the data processing unit; to the DGAF for all activities relating
to itself, the DCBM and DCES; and to the DEPP for the statistics section.
11.       Means.  The project would finance:  (a) minor civil works to
improve office space and adapt existing buildings for the above key
directorates and units; (b) one micro-computer for the data processing unit,
the DGAF, and the DCBM; (c) general office furniture and equipment for the
data processing unit and the DGAF; (d) one light vehicle and six mobylettes
for the DGAF; (e) forty-eight manmonths of locally based in-service training
for existing staff in the three key units and eighteen manmonths of overseas
training in the fields of procurement, inventory of physical facilities,
budgeting systems and education statistics; (f) a total of ten manmonths of
short-term consultant services from foreign specialists and twelve manmonths
of local specialists; and (g) limited incremental operating expenditures for
vehicles and micro-computers.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
E1TW' TION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND RESPONSIBILITIES FOR PROJECT COORDINATION
1.        Background.  Following managerial difficulties experienced with
the second education project Cr. 1359-CA (poor personnel management, misuse
of funds, distortion of project objectives, inefficient procurement
methods, lack of appropriate staff, and inordinate delays in replacing
staff vacancies) due, in part, to confusion between MEN directorates on
their respective roles in the implementation of that project, the
Government has decided to create a small but senior level project
management unit to coordinate all aspects of the implementation of the
project. This decision is in line with the revised organizational
structure of MEN, and will resolve the problems apparent in the previous
project. At the same time the Directorate of Projects and Exterior
Relations has been rationalized and reoriented towards planning functions
and the new Project Coordination Unit (PCU) is being created to manage the
implementation of the project.
2.        Objectives.  The main objective of this component is to ensure
adequate institutional and administrative arrangements to successfully
implement the project, while reinforcing and developing the managerial
capacity of MEN. Intermediate objectives include: (i) supporting the
Government's institutional building policy; (ii) placing project management
at a sufficiently high level in MEN's hierarchy to ensure adequate status
to provide inter departmental and inter ministerial coordination;
(iii) integrating the implementation of the project with the MEN's
organizational framework; and (iv) making individual managerial units
responsible for the implementation of the project components directly
related to them.
3.        Approach/strategy.  To this end, the implementation
responsibility for this project would be assumed by the Project
Coordination Unit (PCU) with the responsibility for individual project
components and sub-components decentralized to the concerned directorates
and departments of the MEN and MESRES. The Project Coordination Unit would
be attached to the Secretary General of MEN, and would be headed by a full
time national Project Director, assisted by a management team, led by a
Deputy Director (T.A.).
4.        Functions.  With responsibility for overall supervision and
guidance of project implementation, the project director would oversee the
coordination of all project activities, including liaison with the
different units of MEN, other ministries and government services concerned
with the project, and would serve as the direct contact point between the
Government and IDA. More specifically, the PCU would:



- 151 -                     ANNEX 3-9
Page 2 of 5
(a) assist each Directorate in the preparation of annual work
programs and budgets for the implementation of each project
component;
(b) prepare all necessary documentation for the annual review of the
Action Plan made jointly by the Government and IDA, and act as
the secretariat of the Government's review committee;
(c) coordinate all aspects of project implementation with the
relevant Government entities;
(d)  ensure that those responsible for each component carry out their
functions effectively;
(e) supervise the preparation of bidding documents and handle the
procurement of civil works, goods and specialist services in line
with IDA's guidelines;
(f) plan and carry out all procurement functions, and ensure that
goods and services procured under the project are delivered to
their destination;
(g) arrange for the recruitment of personnel and specialists required
by the project;
(h) monitor and administer the specialist service contracts for both
qualitative and quantitative conformity with contract obligations
(short term as well as long term) and training/fellowship
programs (local and abroad);
(i) ensure adequate Government counterpart funding is budgeted and
monitor the satisfactory flow of funds for the project components
(from counterpart and IDA sources);
(j) prepare and maintain all project accounts, following accepted
accounting formats and procedures;
(k) prepare all necessary documents for withdrawal of funds from the
local project accounts (Special Account for IDA financing and
separate account for the counterpart funds) and for submission of
withdrawal application to IDA;
(1) arrange for annual and semi-annual audits of all project accounts
by auditors selected following IDA guidelines and ensure that
such audit reports are forwarded to IDA within six months after
the end of eacn fiscal year;
(m)  keep records, including financial records of the project, and
submit to IDA semi-annually progress reports on project
implementation and a completion repert no later than six months
after completion of the project; and
(n) arrange for the preparation of future projects.



- 152 -
ANNEX 3-9
Page 3 of 5
5.        Means.  In order to carry out its responsibilities, the PCU will
be staffed with the following personnel, all adequately qualified and
experienced:
Responsible staff         Status    Functions
(a)  a full time Director      local      project management
(b)  a Deputy Director         foreign   assistant to (a)
(c)  an Administrator          local      financial/contracts management
(d)  an accountant             local      accounting
(e)  support staff             local      (one secretary and one driver)
Additional support staff would be provided on a needs basis by current
civil servants of MEN. In addition, the project would finance the
following specialist services (short term consultants) to assist the PCU in
specific areas:
Profile specialist              Status    Duration (manmonths)
(i) educationalist                  foreign       2   m/m
(ii) procurement specialist          foreign       6   m/m
(iii) financial systems/accounts    foreign         6   m/m
(iv) computer specialist             foreign       2.2 m/m
(v) auditors                        foreign      14   m/m
(vi) review committee members        local        30   m/m
(vii) short term specialists          foreign      18   m/m
(for future project preparation)
Finally, the project makes provision for minor office building repairs and
the necessary office equipment, transport and operating costs for the PCU
during project implementation.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
PROJECT COST AND FINANCING
1.        Basis of Cost Estimates.  Project costs are based on March 1987
prices, updated to reflect a change in the exchange rate from FCFA 314 =
US$1 at appraisal in March/April 1987, to FCFA 305 = US$1 at negotiations
(October 198/). Cost estimates for primary school classroom
rehabilitation/construction are based on the experience of similar
construction completed in 1987 under the previous project and the
development of a prototype of a low-cost classroom using local materials
and labor. Construction cost estimates are based on recent tenders for
similar types of construction with adjustment for regional cost
differences. The average cost per month of internationally recruited
specialist services is estimated at US$12,000 for long-term services and
includes: salary, fees, overhead and subsistence. Unit costs for
international consultants are based on recent proposals from the
International Labor Organization. Internationally recruited volunteer
specialists have been costed at US$900 per manmonth. Scholarships abroad
are costed at US$2,000 per manmonth, based on standards used under UNDP
projects. The cost of locally recruited specialists and consultants,
together with the costing of locally arranged seminars are based on recent
contracts awarded during project preparation (January-May 1987).
2.        Foreign Excnante Component.  The estimated foreign exchange
component of the project is US$11.9 million (572 of the total project
cost), and is the result of the following mission estimated foreign
exchange percentages for the individual categories of expenditure:
Civil workst
Civil works contract                           502
School construction (IDA)                      602
Community inputs (APE) and school
director housing (Government)                   02
Furniture                                        502
Equipment                                       1001
Pedagogical materials and supplies              1002
International specialists                       1002
Local/national specialists                        02
Incremental operating costs                      602
3.        Customs. Duties and Taxes.  Project cost estimates do not include
duties or taxes. During negotiations, the Government confirmed that the
project would be exempt from taxes and duties.
4.        Contingenac  Allowances.  Project cost estimates include physical
contingencies oft (a) 102 for the civil works, furniture, equipment, and
pedagogical materials and supplies; and (b) 52 for specialist services,
training expenditures and incremental operating expenditures. Allowances
for annual price escalations are as follows:



- 156 -
ANNEX 3-10
Page 2 of 6
Projected Annual Price Escalation Rates
1988   1989   1990    1991   1992   1993   1994
Foreign Costs      5.6%   5.4%  -0.3%    1.3%   1.3%   1.3%   1.3%
Local Costs       -1.4%  -0.6%   4.1%    4.0%   4.0%   4.0%   4.0%
At these rates total physical contingencies are estimated at US$1.38
million (6.6% of total project costs) and price contingencies at US$1.58
million (7.6% of total project costs).
5.        Project Cost and Financing Plan.  Table 1 shows the project cost
by category of expenditure and project component. Table 2 shows project
cost by component; Table 3 the project cost by category of expenditure, and
Table 4 shows the financing plan by component.

CENTRAL AFRICAN REPUBLIC
EDUCATION REHABILITATION AND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY OF RECURRENT EXPENDITURES AND OF COST SAVINGS
GENERATED BY THE PROJECT
1.        The table below provides estimates of (a) the incremental
recurrent expenditures, and (b) the cost savings generated by the project.
The assumptions behind these calculations are explained in the notes to the
table. The estimates of incremental expenditures reflect the likely effect
of the project on the Government recurrent budget and are due to: project
related operating expenses (cofinanced by IDA and Government); the
completion of the in-service training program for primary teachers; the
salary cost of the teachers required for the additional classrooms built by
the project; the salary effects of the "concours professionnel" for primary
teachers; primary level textbook replacement after 3.5 years; the continued
operation of the school inspectorates; the preventive maintenance costs for
the buildings constructed by the project; and the effects of a reduction in
the average class size. Estimates of cost savings are more uncertain and
are likely to underestimate the beneficial effects of the project since
they do not include difficult quantifiable effects such as the impact of
improved institutional capabilities on the cost-effectiveness of resource
use, the impact of better trained teachers and improved availability,
relevance and quality of textbooks on internal efficiency of primary
education, and the effects of increased contributions by local communities
to school construction made possible by the development of low-cost
construction techniques based on locally available materials.
Nevertheless, the estimate is based on the effects of: the purge of the
scholarship lists; the planned reduction in the scholarship budget; the
effects of the staffing plan (redeployment, redundancies and retirements);
and the planned increase in the number of hours taught by teachers.
2.        The estimates of cost savings assume an annual enrollment growth
rate of 3%. This equals the projected growth in the school-age population
over the period considered and would imply maintaining the primary school
gross enrollment ratio close to its 1987 level (78%). The estimates show
that the savings resulting from the measures included in the project are
likely to substantially exceed the recurrent expenditures generated,
especially when the project is fully developed.
